Conditional Tadbir with a Time Limit: The Ghaya Exclusion Principle
Ruling on tadbir muqayyad with time limit and analysis of ghaya inclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the ghaya (limit) in conditional tadbir excludes its endpoint, and determine when the slave is freed.

Core Idea
In tadbir, 'until a year' excludes the endpoint; the slave is freed at death within the period, not after.

This lesson examines a specific conditional tadbir where the master sets a time limit, such as 'if I die within a year.' The key issue is whether the limit (ghaya) includes the endpoint. The Hanafi school, using analogy with oaths, holds that the ghaya is excluded, so freedom occurs before the period ends. This precision affects when the slave becomes free and has practical implications for inheritance and contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a conditional tadbir (tadbir muqayyad) with a time condition. The ruling is that the tadbir is valid only if the master dies within the specified period. If the master dies after the period, the tadbir is void and the slave is not freed.
2
Locate the dalil
The author uses analogy (qiyas) with oaths to prove the ruling. In oaths, the ghaya (e.g., 'until tomorrow') excludes the endpoint, so the oath ends at the start of tomorrow. Here, 'to a year' excludes the year's end.
The analogy is based on the principle of ghayat al-isqat (ghaya of exclusion).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The wajh is that the ghaya in both oaths and tadbir serves to exclude what comes after it. The Hanafi school consistently applies this rule: the endpoint is not included in the condition.
This is proven by the example: 'I will not speak to him until tomorrow' – he may speak tomorrow.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf and tarjih
There is a differing view (al-wajh) that the slave is freed at the end of the period if the master dies exactly at the beginning. The author rejects this as inconsistent because it contradicts the established rule on ghaya in oaths.
The mufta bihi is the majority view: freedom occurs at the moment of death within the period, not at the period's end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد بقوله ويعتق إذا وجد الشرط» أنه لا بد يموت في سفره هذا أو مرضه هذا أو في المدة المعينة . فلو أقام أو صح أو مضت المدة ثم مات لم يعتق لبطلان اليمين قبل الموت.
وفي فتح القدير : من التدبير المقيد أن يقول إن مت إلى سنة فأنت قبل السنة عتق ،مدبراً وإن مات المولى بعد السنة لا يعتق ومقتضى الوجه كونه لو مات في رأس السنة يعتق لأن الغاية هنا لولاها تناول الكلام ما بعدها لأنه يتنجز عتقه فيصير حراً بعد السنة فتكون للإسقاط .اهـ.
وجوابه أن هذا الوجه ليس بمطرد لانتقاضه باليمين في قوله لا أكلمه إلى غد فإن الغاية لا تدخل في ظاهر الرواية فله أن يكلمه في الغد مع أنها غاية إسقاط، وكذلك أكلت السمكة إلى رأسها لا تدخل الغاية مع أنه للإسقاط .
And he benefited by his saying, 'and he is freed if the condition is found' that it is necessary that he dies in this journey of his, or this illness of his, or in the specified period. So if he stays or recovers or the period passes and then he dies, he is not freed because the oath becomes void before death. And in Fath al-Qadir: Among restricted tadbir is that he says 'if I die within a year, you are free before the year' – he becomes a mudabbar. If the master dies after the year, he is not freed. The implication of the aspect is that if he dies at the beginning of the year, he is freed because the ghaya (limit) here, were it not for it, the statement would include what comes after it, because his emancipation becomes effective and he becomes free after the year, so it is for exclusion. End. And the answer is that this aspect is not consistent because it is refuted by the oath in his saying 'I will not speak to him until tomorrow'—the ghaya is not included in the apparent narration, so he may speak to him tomorrow, even though it is a ghaya of exclusion. Similarly, 'I ate the fish up to its head'—the ghaya is not included, even though it is for exclusion.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and a critical refinement regarding tadbir muqayyad with a time limit. The author clarifies that if the master says, 'If I die within this journey/illness/year,' the condition must occur within that time. If the master survives the journey, recovers, or the year passes, then the ta'liq (oath) becomes void, and the slave is not freed even if the master dies later. The author then quotes Fath al-Qadir discussing the case 'if I die within a year, you are free before the year' – this phrasing creates ambiguity: does the slave become free at the end of the year or at the moment of death within the year? The text mentions a 'aspect' that the word 'ila' (until) might exclude the end point, but the author rejects this as inconsistent, citing the Hanafi principle that in oaths of exclusion (ghayat al-isqat), the ghaya is not included. Example: if one swears 'I will not speak to him until tomorrow,' he may speak to him tomorrow because the ghaya (tomorrow) is excluded. Similarly, in tadbir, 'to a year' means the slave is freed before the year, not after. This is an important precision for determining when freedom takes effect.

Key Terms
غاية إسقاط (ghayat al-isqat): A limit (ghaya) that excludes what comes after it, typically used in oaths and declarations to indicate cessation.
تدبير مقيد (tadbir muqayyad): A conditional declaration of emancipation that is tied to a specific condition, such as a time limit or a particular event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says to his slave: 'If I die within this year, you are free before the year.' The master dies at the beginning of the next year. What is the ruling?
A) The slave is freed because the master died.
B) The slave is not freed because the condition was not met within the year.
C) The slave is freed at the end of the year.
D) The tadbir is void because the phrasing is contradictory.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tadbir with a time limit, the limit excludes its endpoint; freedom occurs at death within the period, not after.

Linguistic Precision in Tadbir: 'Min Maradi' vs 'Fi Maradi'
Distinction in tadbir when illness is expressed with 'min' vs 'fi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings triggered by 'min maradi' versus 'fi maradi' in a tadbir declaration and apply the correct consequence when the master dies by a cause other than the illness.

Core Idea
Two prepositions, two rulings: 'min' demands causal death, 'fi' only temporal overlap.

This lesson examines a subtle but consequential linguistic distinction in Hanafi tadbir law. The choice between the prepositions 'min' (from) and 'fi' (in) when linking death to an illness determines whether the slave is freed if the master dies by an unrelated cause. Hanafi jurists, especially Imam Muhammad, also clarify that a change in the illness's form does not break its continuity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two formulations
Note the two phrases: 'min maradi' (from my illness) and 'fi maradi' (in my illness). They appear in conditional tadbir clauses.
The preposition is the key variable.
2
Understand the causal requirement
For 'min maradi', the illness must be the direct cause of death. If the master is killed, he does not die from the illness, so the condition fails.
The slave is not freed.
3
Contrast with 'fi maradi'
For 'fi maradi', it is sufficient that death occurs while the master is suffering from the illness, regardless of the immediate cause. Thus, being killed still qualifies.
The slave is freed.
4
Apply the illness continuity rule
Imam Muhammad rules that if the illness transforms (e.g., fever becomes headache), it is still considered the same illness for the purpose of the condition.
Continuity is maintained despite symptomatic change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المجتبى : إن مت من مرضي هذا فهو حر فقتل لا يعتق بخلاف ما لو قال في مرضي، ولو قال إن مت من مرضي وبه حمى فتحول صداعاً أو على عكسه قال محمد : هو مرض واحد اهـ.
And in al-Mujtaba: 'If I die from this illness of mine, he is free'—then if he is killed, he is not freed, unlike if he said 'in my illness'. And if he said 'If I die from my illness' and he had a fever which turned into a headache, or vice versa, Muhammad said: It is one illness. End.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the precision of language in tadbir. The key distinction is between the prepositions 'min' (from) and 'fi' (in). If the master says 'if I die from this illness' (min maradi), the cause of death must be the illness itself. If the master is killed, he does not die from the illness, so the condition is not met and the slave is not freed. However, if he said 'in this illness' (fi maradi), the condition is broader—death while suffering from the illness suffices, even if caused by something else (like being killed). Additionally, Imam Muhammad rules that if the illness changes in nature (e.g., fever turns to headache), it is still considered the same illness for the purpose of the condition. This shows the Hanafi attention to linguistic precision in conditional declarations.

Key Terms
من مرضي (min maradi): A phrase indicating that death must be caused by the illness; a causal connection is required.
في مرضي (fi maradi): A phrase indicating death while suffering from the illness, regardless of the direct cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says: 'If I die from this illness, you are free.' He is then killed by an enemy while still ill. What happens?
A) The slave is freed because the master died during the illness.
B) The slave is not freed because death was not caused by the illness.
C) The slave is freed only if the killing was accidental.
D) The slave becomes a mudabbar but is not freed immediately.

Key Takeaway
The preposition in a tadbir condition determines whether the slave is freed upon non-illness death: 'min' requires causation, 'fi' only coexistence.

Combining Tadbir and Wasiyya for Multiple Slaves
Ruling on a master who declares both slaves free and bequeaths money to each

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a bequest made to multiple slaves under tadbir and distinguish between ambiguous and specified wording.

Core Idea
An ambiguous bequest to multiple slaves spreads equally; a separate bequest to each slave is partly void because a slave cannot receive from his own owner.

This short passage from al-Walwaliji, a later Hanafi fatwa collection, addresses a specific scenario combining two legal constructs: tadbir (declaration of freedom after death) and wasiyya (bequest). The ruling illustrates how the principle of tashayyu' (spreading) applies when the master's words are ambiguous, and the restriction that a bequest to one's own slave is invalid. Understanding this helps clarify the boundaries between valid and void bequests in estate planning for owned persons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The master says either ambiguous 'to him' (singular) or explicit 'to each of you'. Recognize that wording determines the outcome.
Ambiguous wording triggers spreading; explicit wording triggers partial invalidity.
2
Apply the principle of tashayyu' (spreading)
For ambiguous wording, the bequest spreads over both slaves proportionally, so they share the 100 dirhams equally.
Tashayyu' is a Hanafi principle in this context.
3
Analyse the explicit separate bequest
When the master specifies separate amounts to each slave, one of the two bequests is void because a bequest to one's own slave is invalid.
A bequest to a slave is treated as a bequest to the master, and the master cannot bequeath to himself.
4
Derive the mufta bihi ruling
The operative ruling: ambiguous wording leads to sharing; explicit wording leads to one bequest void. This is from al-Walwaliji.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الوالوالجي : رجل قال لعبديه أحد كما حر بعد موتي وأوصيت له بمائة درهم ثم مات عتقاً ولهما المائة بينهما لأنه لما مات شاع العتق فيهما فتشيع الوصية أيضاً، ولو قال لكل واحد منهما مائة درهم تبطل إحدى المائتين لأنها وقعت لعبده اهـ.
And al-Walwaliji mentioned: A man said to his two slaves, 'Each of you is free after my death and I have bequeathed one hundred dirhams to him.' Then he died; they are freed and they share the one hundred dirhams between them because when he died, freedom spread over them, so the will also spreads. If he said, 'To each of you one hundred dirhams,' then one of the two hundreds becomes void because it was given to his slave (i.e., the other). End.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding a combination of tadbir and wasiyya (bequest) for two slaves. If the master declares both slaves free after his death and bequeaths a total of 100 dirhams to them (using singular 'lahu' or ambiguous wording), then upon his death, both are freed and they share the 100 dirhams equally. The freedom spreads (tashayyu') over both, so the bequest also spreads proportionally. However, if he explicitly says 'to each of you one hundred dirhams' (making two separate bequests), then one of the two hundreds is invalid because a bequest to one's own slave (who is owned at the time of death until freed from the estate) is not valid—it goes to the master himself. Since both are slaves, each bequest to each slave is actually a bequest to the master's own property, which is not valid. So one hundred is void. This shows the interplay between the rules of tadbir and wasiyya.

Key Terms
تشييع الوصية (tashayyu' al-wasiyya): The principle that a bequest spreads over all beneficiaries if the wording is general.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says to his two slaves: 'Each of you is free after my death, and I bequeath one hundred dirhams to each of you.' How many dirhams are validly bequeathed?
A) 200 dirhams, shared equally
B) 100 dirhams, shared equally
C) 100 dirhams, given to one slave
D) 0 dirhams, both void

Key Takeaway
An ambiguous bequest to multiple slaves under tadbir is shared; a specific separate bequest to each results in one being void because a bequest to a slave is invalid.

Umm Walad: Definition and Prohibition of Sale
Definition and ruling on umm walad status with proof and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of umm walad, explain why she cannot be sold using the Hanafi proof, and identify the conditions for establishing her status.

Core Idea
A slave who bears her master's child cannot be sold and is freed upon his death.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the umm walad and establishes her unique legal status. You will learn the hadith proof, the legal reasoning based on the mixing of fluids (ma'i), and the conditions that must be met for a female slave to acquire this status, including the type of birth and the master's acknowledgment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the main ruling: the umm walad cannot be sold. Note that she becomes free after the master's death, but the right to freedom is established immediately, which prohibits her sale.
The ruling is from the hadith 'Her child frees her'.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
Find the hadith cited: 'Her child frees her' (أعتقها ولدها). This is a prophetic statement that informs us of her emancipation, establishing some of its requisites, namely the prohibition of sale.
The dalil is naqli (textual).
3
Understand the ta'lil (legal reasoning)
Analyze the reasoning: the mixing of the master's and slave's fluids (ma'i) through the child creates a partiality (juz'iyya) that renders sale prohibited, similar to the prohibition in marriage affinity. The cause is weak, so the freedom is deferred until after death.
The partiality remains legally (hukman) even after separation.
4
Determine what constitutes a 'child'
Identify the conditions: the child must be live-born, stillborn, or a miscarriage with some limbs formed. If no limbs form, she does not become umm walad even if the master claims it.
Rulings of childbirth (idda, nifas) attach to a formed stillbirth.
5
Apply the acknowledgment rules
Examine the text's discussion of acknowledgment: statements like 'her pregnancy is from me' or 'she is pregnant from me' establish the status irrevocably. Ambiguous statements like 'what is in her womb is from me' without specifying pregnancy or child may not suffice if later retracted.
Precise language matters because freedom involves a right of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الاستيلاد
ولدت أمة السيد لم تملك لقوله عليه السلام أعتقها ولدها أخبر عن إعتاقها فيثبت بعض موجبه وهو حرمة البيع، ولأن الجزئية قد حصلت بين الواطىء والموطوءة بواسطة الولد فإن المائين قد اختلطا بحيث لا يمكن الميز بينهما على ما عرف في حرمة المصاهرة إلا أن بعد الانفصال تبقى الجزئية حكماً لا حقيقة فضعف السبب فأوجب حكماً مؤجلاً إلى ما بعد الموت وبقاء الجزئية حكماً باعتبار النسب وهو من جانب الرجال، فكذا الحرية تثبت في حقهم لا في حقهن حتى إذا ملكت الحرة زوجها وقد ولدت منه لم يعتق بموتها، وبثبوت عتق مؤجل يثبت حق الحرية في الحال فيمتنع جواز البيع وإخراجها لا إلى الحرية في الحال ويوجب عتقها بعد موته أطلق في الولد فشمل الولد الحي والميت لأن الميت ولد بدليل أنه يتعلق به أحكام الولادة حتى تنقضي به العدة وتصير المرأة نفساء، وشمل السقط الذي استبان بعض خلقه فإن لم يستبن شيء لا تكون أم ولد وإن ادعاه المولى. ولو قال المصنف أمة من السيد مكان ولدت لكان أولى لما في البدائع والمحيط والخانية: لو قال لجاريته حملها مني صارت أم ولد له لأن الإقرار بالحمل إقرار بالولد، وكذا لو قال حبلى مني أو ما في بطنها من ولد فهو مني ولا يقبل منه بعده أنها لم تكن حاملاً وإنما كان ريحاً ولو صدقته الأمة لأن في الحرية حق الله تعالى فلا يحتمل السقوط بإسقاط العبد بخلاف ما إذا قال ما في بطنها مني ولم يقل من حمل أو ولد ثم قال بعده كان ريحاً وصدقته لم تصر أم ولد لاحتمال الولد والريح ولو قال إن كانت حبلى فهو مني فأسقطت ...
Chapter of Istilad: A female slave of the master gives birth: she is not owned (i.e., cannot be sold) because of his saying, peace be upon him, 'Her child frees her.' He informed about her emancipation, so some of its requisites are established, which is the prohibition of sale. And because partiality (juz'iyya) has occurred between the penetrator and the penetrated through the child, for the two waters have mixed in a way that distinction between them is impossible, as is known in the prohibition of marriage affinity, except that after separation, the partiality remains legally (hukman) not actually (haqiqa). So the cause is weak and necessitates a ruling postponed until after death. And the remaining partiality legally is by virtue of lineage, which is from the male side. Thus freedom is established in their right (the children) not in her right (the mother). So if a free woman owns her husband and has given birth from him, he is not freed by her death. And by the establishment of deferred freedom, a right to freedom is established instantly, so the permissibility of sale is prevented, and she is not taken out to freedom immediately, but it necessitates her freedom after his death. He (the author) made 'al-walad' (the child) general, so it includes the living child and the dead child, because the dead child is (still) a child, evidenced by the fact that rulings of childbirth are attached to it: the waiting period (idda) ends with it and the woman becomes nifas (postpartum). And it includes the miscarriage in which some of its limbs have formed; if nothing has formed, she does not become umm walad, even if the master claims it. And if the author had said 'a slave woman of the master' instead of 'gives birth' it would have been better, because in al-Bada'i', al-Muhit, and al-Khaniyya: if he said to his concubine, 'her pregnancy is from me,' she becomes his umm walad, because acknowledgment of pregnancy is acknowledgment of birth. Similarly, if he said, 'pregnant from me' or 'what is in her womb is a child and it is from me,' and later he cannot claim that she was not pregnant but it was just wind, even if the female slave confirms him, because freedom involves a right of Allah, so it does not admit of dropping by the dropping of the slave. Unlike if he said, 'what is in her womb is from me' without saying 'pregnancy' or 'child', then later said it was wind and she confirmed him, she does not become umm walad because of the possibility of both child and wind. And if he said, 'If she is pregnant, it is from me,' and she miscarried...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling regarding the status of umm walad (a female slave who bears her master's child). It begins by stating the core ruling: she cannot be sold. The proof is the hadith 'her child frees her' (referring to eventual freedom after the master's death), and the legal reasoning (ta'lil) based on the mixing of fluids (ma'i) creating a partiality that prohibits sale, similar to marriage affinity. The passage clarifies that freedom is deferred until after the master's death, but the right to freedom is established immediately, which prohibits her sale. It also discusses what constitutes a 'child': includes live births, stillbirths, and miscarriages with formed limbs; if no limbs formed, she does not become umm walad even if the master claims. The passage further discusses acknowledgment: if the master says 'her pregnancy is from me' or 'she is pregnant from me', she becomes umm walad, and he cannot later retract. However, if he says 'what is in her womb is from me' without specifying pregnancy or child, and later says it was wind and she confirms, she does not become umm walad because the statement is ambiguous. This shows the importance of precise language in establishing the status.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to her master's child, thus acquiring the right to freedom upon his death.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of seeking a child from one's female slave; legally, it refers to the status of the slave becoming umm walad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master says to his concubine: 'What is in your womb is from me.' Later he says it was just wind, and she confirms. Does she become umm walad?
A) Yes, because he acknowledged it.
B) No, because the statement was ambiguous and could refer to wind.
C) Yes, because the concubine confirmed the wind.
D) No, because only birth establishes umm walad.

Key Takeaway
The umm walad's special status—freedom after master's death and prohibition of sale—is founded on the hadith and the legal reasoning of partiality through mixed fluids, and it requires a formed child and clear acknowledgment.

Why the Umm Walad Cannot Be Sold
Commentary expanding on the matn ruling and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling an umm walad, derive it from the hadith and rational argument, and identify the conditions for her status.

Core Idea
The umm walad cannot be sold because her child establishes a right to deferred freedom.

The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the matn 'a female slave gives birth, she is not owned'. It provides the proof from hadith and a rational argument based on fluid mixture. The commentator also expands on conditions for istilad and the scope of the prohibition. This lesson unpacks the key rulings and their foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage opens with the ruling that an umm walad 'is not owned', meaning sale and transfer are prohibited. Determine the hukm level (haram) and the legal effect.
The prohibition is immediate, though freedom is deferred.
2
Locate the primary proof
The hadith 'Her child frees her' (أعتقها ولدها) is cited. Examine how this hadith supports the prohibition of sale.
It is a zanni thubut and dalala hadith, but sufficient for wujub.
3
Examine the rational argument
The commentator adds a qiyas argument: the mixing of fluids creates a partial integration, which gives the umm walad a legal claim to freedom, thus preventing sale. Trace the illa.
This is unique Hanafi reasoning.
4
List the conditions for istilad
The passage details conditions: child must be fully or partially formed, acknowledgment of lineage is required, master can be non-Muslim, etc. Identify each condition.
A miscarriage without formed limbs does not create umm walad status.
5
Determine the scope of the prohibition
The prohibition covers sale, gift, pledge, and any removal from ownership. However, service and intercourse remain permissible as she is still owned.
This distinguishes her from a free woman.

قوله : (ولدت أمة السيد لم تملك) لقوله عليه السلام «أعتقها ولدها» أخبر عن إعتاقها فيثبت بعض مواجبه وهو حرمة البيع، ولأن الجزئية قد حصلت بين الواطئ والموطوءة بواسطة الولد فإن المائين قد اختلطا بحيث لا يمكن الميز بينهما على ما عرف في حرمة المصاهرة إلا أن بعد الانفصال تبقى الجزئية حكماً لا حقيقة فضعف السبب فأوجب حكماً مؤجلاً إلى ما بعد الموت وبقاء الجزئية حكماً باعتبار النسب وهو من جانب الرجال، فكذا الحرية تثبت في حقهم لا في حقهن حتى إذا ملكت الحرة زوجها وقد ولدت منه لم يعتق بموتها، وبثبوت عتق مؤجل يثبت حق الحرية في الحال فيمتنع جواز البيع وإخراجها لا إلى الحرية في الحال ويوجب عتقها بعد موته. أطلق في الولد فشمل الولد الحي والميت لأن الميت ولد بدليل أنه يتعلق به أحكام الولادة حتى تنقضي به العدة وتصير المرأة نفساء، وشمل السقط الذي استبان بعض خلقه فإن لم يستبن شيء لا تكون أم ولد وإن ادعاه المولى. ولو قال المصنف أمة من السيد مكان ولدت لكان أولى لما في البدائع والمحيط والخانية: لو قال لجاريته حملها مني صارت أم ولد له لأن الإقرار بالحمل إقرار بالولد، وكذا لو قال حبلى مني أو ما في بطنها من ولد فهو مني ولا يقبل منه بعده أنها لم تكن حاملاً وإنما كان ريحاً ولو صدقته الأمة لأن في الحرية حق الله تعالى فلا يحتمل السقوط بإسقاط العبد بخلاف ما إذا قال ما في بطنها مني ولم يقل من حمل أو ولد ثم قال بعده كان ريحاً وصدقته لم تصر أم ولد لاحتمال الولد والريح ولو قال إن كانت حبلى فهو مني فأسقطت مستبين الخلق كله أو بعضه صارت أم ولد فإن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر صارت أم ولد للتيقن بحملها حينئذ وإن ولدته لأكثر لم تصر أم ولد اهـ. وأطلق في الولادة من السيد فشمل ما إذا كان بجماع منه أو بغيره لما في المحيط عن أبي حنيفة : إذا عالج الرجل جاريته فيما دون الفرج فأنزل فأخذت الجارية ماءه في شيء فاستدخلته فرجها في حدثان ذلك فعلقت الجارية وولدت فالولد ولده والجارية أم ولد له .اهـ. وأفاد بالولادة من السيد أنه لا بد من ثبوت النسب منه أولاً لتصير أم ولد له فإن السبب عندنا وثبوت النسب منه موقوف على إقراره كما سيأتي. وبه اندفع ما في فتح القدير من أنهم أخلوا بقيد ثبوت النسب لأن الولادة منه لا تتحقق إلا بالاعتراف فلا إحلال خصوصاً قد صرحوا به بعد. وأطلق في السيد فشمل ما إذا كان سيدها وقت الولادة أو لا حتى لو تزوج جارية إنسان فاستولدها ثم ملكها صارت أم ولد له لأن سبب الاستيلاء ثبوت النسب بخلاف ما إذا زنى بجارية إنسان فولدت ثم ملكها لعدم ثبوت النسب. وشمل ما إذا كان مالكاً كلها أو بعضها لأن الاستيلاد لا يتجزئ فإنه فرع النسب فيعتبر بأصله، وشمل السيد المسلم والكافر ذمياً أو مرتداً أو مستأمناً، كذا في البدائع. وأطلق الأمة فشمل القنة والمدبرة لاستوائهما في إثبات النسب إلا أن المدبرة إذا صارت أم ولد بطل الكتابة استعجال حقها في العتق متى أدت البدل قبل موت المولى فلم تتضمن هذه التصرفات إبطال حقها وملكه قائم فيها فصحت .اهـ. فإنه على ما في البدائع ينبغي أن لا يصح التدبير فإن الاستيلاد أقوى منه ولا فائدة فيه معه . وفي الذخيرة معنى قوله بطل التدبير أنه لا يظهر حكم التدبير بعد ذلك فكأنه بطل لأنها تعتق من جميع المال. وأفاد بقوله «لم تملك» أنه لا يجوز بيعها ولا هبتها ولا إخراجها عن الملك بوجه وكذا لا يجوز رهنها وليس المراد أنها لم تملك لأحد لأنها باقية على ملك مولاها بدليل ما سيأتي من جواز وطئها.
His statement: (A female slave of the master gives birth, she is not owned) due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Her child frees her.' He informed about her emancipation, so some of its requirements are established, which is the prohibition of sale. And because partial integration occurred between the penetrator and the penetrated via the child, for the two waters have mixed such that distinction between them is impossible, as known in the prohibition of affinity, except that after separation the partial integration remains legally not actually, so the cause was weak and necessitated a deferred ruling until after death, and the partial integration remains legally with regard to lineage which is from the male side. Thus freedom is established in their right, not in their right, such that if a free woman owns her husband and has given birth from him, he is not emancipated upon her death. And by the establishment of deferred emancipation, the right to freedom is established immediately, so selling is prohibited, and she is not removed [from ownership] except to freedom immediately, and it necessitates her emancipation after his death. He made 'child' unrestricted, so it includes the living and dead child, because the dead child is a child as evidenced by the fact that birth rulings apply to it, so the waiting period is completed and the woman becomes postpartum. And it includes a miscarriage that has some of its limbs formed; if nothing is formed, she does not become an umm walad even if the master claims it. And if the author had said 'a female slave of the master' instead of 'gives birth,' it would have been better, as in al-Bada'i', al-Muhit, and al-Khaniyya: If he said to his slave girl, 'Her pregnancy is from me,' she becomes an umm walad for him because acknowledging pregnancy is acknowledging the child. Similarly, if he said 'pregnant from me' or 'what is in her belly is a child from me,' and later he cannot claim that she was not pregnant but only wind, even if the slave girl confirms him, because in freedom there is the right of Allah, so it cannot fall by the slave's dropping. Unlike if he said 'what is in her belly is from me' without saying 'pregnancy' or 'child,' then later he said it was wind and she confirmed him, she does not become umm walad due to the possibility of child or wind. And if he said, 'If she is pregnant, then it is from me,' and she miscarried a creature with some or all limbs formed, she becomes umm walad. If she gives birth in less than six months, she becomes umm walad due to certainty of pregnancy at that time; if she gives birth after more, she does not become umm walad. And he made 'giving birth from the master' unrestricted, so it includes whether by intercourse or otherwise, as in al-Muhit from Abu Hanifa: If a man treats his slave girl with foreplay and ejaculates, and the slave girl takes his fluid in something and inserts it into her vagina immediately, and she becomes pregnant and gives birth, the child is his and the slave girl is his umm walad. And he indicated by 'giving birth from the master' that lineage from him must be established first for her to become his umm walad, for the cause in our school is that lineage from him depends on his acknowledgment, as will come. And with this is refuted what is in Fath al-Qadir that they omitted the condition of establishing lineage, because giving birth from him is not realized except by acknowledgment, so there is no omission, especially since they stated it later. And he made 'master' unrestricted, so it includes whether he was her master at the time of birth or not, so that even if he married the slave girl of someone and had a child from her then owned her, she becomes his umm walad, because the cause of istilad is establishing lineage, unlike if he committed adultery with a slave girl of someone then owned her after she gave birth, due to lack of lineage. And it includes whether he owns all or part of her, because istilad cannot be divided, for it is a branch of lineage and is considered according to its origin. And it includes the Muslim and disbelieving master, whether dhimmi, apostate, or musta'min, as in al-Bada'i'. And he made 'slave girl' unrestricted, so it includes both the absolute slave (qinn) and the mudabbara, because they are equal in establishing lineage, except that if the mudabbara becomes an umm walad, the tadbir becomes invalid because being an umm walad is more beneficial for her as she is not required to work; as in al-Bada'i'. But this is problematic given what is in al-Muhit that it is permissible to emancipate her, make her a mudabbara, or write a kitaba for her, because in emancipation there is expediting her right, in tadbir there is combining causes of freedom, and in kitaba there is hastening her right to freedom if she pays the substitute before the master's death, and these acts do not entail invalidating her right, and his ownership remains valid over her, so they are valid. According to al-Bada'i', tadbir should not be valid because istilad is stronger and there is no benefit in it with istilad. And in al-Dhakhira, the meaning of his statement 'tadbir becomes invalid' is that the ruling of tadbir does not appear after that, as if it were invalid because she is emancipated from all the estate. And he indicated by his statement 'she is not owned' that selling her, gifting her, or removing her from ownership in any way is not permissible, and likewise pledging her is not permissible. And it is not meant that she is not owned by anyone, because she remains in the ownership of her master, as evidenced by what will come about the permissibility of intercourse with her.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq on the chapter of istilad (making a slave girl an umm walad). The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) explains the matn phrase by phrase, providing proof and detailing conditions. The first ruling is that when a female slave gives birth to her master's child, she becomes an umm walad, and as a consequence, she cannot be sold, gifted, or removed from ownership in any way. The proof is the hadith 'Her child frees her,' which indicates that the child is a cause of her freedom, and one of the requirements of that freedom is the prohibition of sale. Additionally, the commentator provides a rational argument: the mixing of the fluids creates a partial integration between the master and the slave girl through the child, which gives her a legal claim to freedom, albeit deferred until the master's death. This claim makes her unsellable immediately. The text then clarifies conditions for becoming an umm walad: the child must be born alive or dead with some formed limbs; a miscarriage without any formed limbs does not make her an umm walad even if the master claims it. The commentator also discusses the preferred wording of the matn, suggesting that 'a female slave of the master' is better than 'gives birth' because the ruling can also be established by acknowledgment of pregnancy. He then discusses cases of acknowledgment: if the master acknowledges that the pregnancy is from him, she becomes an umm walad, and his later denial is not accepted. The passage further expands on the term 'from the master,' including cases where conception occurs by intercourse or even by insertion of his reproductive fluid. It stresses that lineage must be established for her to be umm walad, which is done through his acknowledgment. The term 'master' includes anyone who owns her at the time of birth or later, including non-Muslims. The term 'slave girl' includes both absolute slaves (qinn) and mudabbara, though there is a discussion about whether tadbir remains valid after istilad, with the relied-upon position that it is invalid because umm walad status is stronger. Finally, the prohibition of sale is detailed: she cannot be sold, given as a gift, pledged, or removed from ownership in any way, but she remains the master's property and can be used for service and intercourse.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who gives birth to a child from her master, establishing lineage, and thereby acquires a right to deferred emancipation upon the master's death.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master fathering a child on his slave girl, thereby giving her the status of umm walad.
عتق مؤجل (itq mu'ajjal): Deferred emancipation; the freedom of the umm walad is postponed until the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition must a miscarriage satisfy for the slave girl to become an umm walad?
A) It must occur after six months of pregnancy.
B) It must have some formed limbs (مستبين الخلق).
C) The master must claim it as his child, regardless of formation.
D) The miscarriage must be caused by the master's actions.

Key Takeaway
The umm walad cannot be sold because her child establishes a deferred right to freedom, supported by the hadith and the rational argument of fluid mixture, subject to conditions of lineage and child formation.

The Master's Claim for Paternity of a Slave Woman's Child
Discussion on da'wa requirement and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the necessity of the master's claim for establishing paternity of his slave woman's child, distinguish between the requirement for court adjudication and the internal moral obligation, and apply the conditions that affect the ruling.

Core Idea
The master's claim is required only for court; internally, he must acknowledge the child if he intended offspring and guarded her.

In Hanafi law, the paternity of a child born to a slave woman is not automatically established like that of a wife. The master's explicit claim (da'wa) plays a crucial role, but its necessity differs depending on context. This lesson unpacks the ruling by examining the conditions and intra-Hanafi discussions from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, clarifying the distinction between legal proof in court and the truth before Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Determine the legal act in question: the master's claim that the child of his slave woman is his offspring. Identify the ruling for court adjudication: the claim is wajib (necessary) to establish paternity and umm walad status. However, note that between the master and Allah, acknowledgment may be required without a formal claim.
The passage emphasizes that the ruling is not absolute; it depends on context (e.g., whether the master practiced 'azl or guarded the woman).
2
Examine the Proof (Dalil)
The Hanafi proof is based on transmitted texts (naqli) from earlier Hanafi authorities like al-Bada'i', al-Hidaya, and Fath al-Qadir. The school's position is that a slave woman's bed (firash) is weaker than a wife's, so a claim is needed to overcome the default assumption. The proof also includes the analogy (qiyas) to cases where the woman becomes forbidden (e.g., by marriage or kitaba).
No single Quranic verse or hadith is quoted; the dalil is the established consensus of the school, supported by reasoning and precedent.
3
Analyze the Conditions
The passage lists conditions that affect whether paternity is established without a claim: the woman being forbidden (permanently or temporarily), the master's practice of 'azl (coitus interruptus), and tahseen (confining the woman to prevent suspicion). Understanding these conditions is essential to applying the ruling correctly.
For example, if the woman is permanently forbidden (e.g., by being married to another), paternity requires a claim. If she is temporarily forbidden (e.g., due to menstruation), no claim is needed.
4
Consider the Intra-Hanafi Difference
Some Hanafi scholars, particularly those cited in Fath al-Qadir, argue that if the master acknowledges he did not practice 'azl and guarded the woman, paternity is established without a claim even in court. However, the majority position (mufta bihi) holds that the claim remains necessary for court adjudication.
This difference stems from a conflict between apparent (zahir) signs and the need for explicit declaration.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that da'wa is required for court adjudication. Internally, the master must acknowledge the child if he intended offspring and took precautions. This distinction is critical for fatwa.
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq leans towards the majority view but acknowledges the strength of the alternative opinion.

If he claims the lineage of the oldest, the lineage of the oldest is established from him, and the middle and youngest take the status of the mother; their lineage is not established, and he may not sell them because it is incumbent upon him by law to acknowledge the lineage of a child that is from him. Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing the chapter on umm walad (a slave woman who bears her master's child). The central issue is the requirement of the master's explicit claim (da'wa) to establish his paternity of the child and consequently make the woman an umm walad. The text examines various scenarios and authorities, ultimately distinguishing between the evidentiary requirement in court (where da'wa is necessary) and the moral obligation before Allah (where the master must acknowledge the child if he intended offspring and took precautions against other possibilities, such as coitus interruptus or allowing her to go out). The author quotes from al-Zahiriyya, al-Bada'i', Fath al-Qadir, al-Hidaya, and other sources. He discusses cases where prohibition (like marriage or kitaba) affects the ruling, as well as cases involving multiple children and the effect of silence over time. The passage also addresses the exceptional case of an insane or mentally deficient master, where da'wa is impossible but istilad is still valid, though this is considered problematic and requires further reflection. The overall ruling reflects the Hanafi position that paternity for a slave woman's child requires the master's claim for legal adjudication, but internally the master must be truthful.

Key Terms
الدعوة (ad-da'wa): The master's explicit verbal claim that the child born to his slave woman is his offspring.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child recognized as her master's; she cannot be sold and becomes free upon her master's death.
التحصين (at-tahseen): The master preventing the slave woman from leaving the house and from situations that could lead to suspicion (riyba).
العزل (al-'azl): Coitus interruptus; the master withdrawing before ejaculation to prevent pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A master had intercourse with his slave woman during a period when she was temporarily forbidden due to ihram (pilgrimage state). She gave birth after six months. According to the Hanafi school as discussed in this passage, what is required for the paternity to be established in court?
A) The master must make a claim (da'wa) because any prohibition weakens the bed.
B) No claim is needed because temporary prohibition does not change the ruling of the bed.
C) A claim is needed only if the master practiced 'azl.
D) Paternity cannot be established because ihram prohibits intercourse.

Key Takeaway
The master's claim (da'wa) is necessary for court adjudication of paternity for a slave woman's child, but internally the master must acknowledge the child if he intended offspring and guarded her; the distinction between legal and moral obligation is central.

Requirement of the Master's Claim for Lineage of Umm al-Walad's Child
Explaining the condition of da'wa with distinction between judicial and divine reality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition of the master's claim (da'wa) for establishing lineage and distinguish between its applicability in judicial versus divine reality.

Core Idea
The master's claim is necessary for judicial recognition, but not for the child's status before Allah.

In Hanafi law, the lineage of a child born to a slave woman depends on the master's explicit claim (da'wa). However, this requirement is nuanced: it applies to the legal sphere (judicial proof), not to the inner reality between the master and Allah. If the circumstances indicate paternity, the master must acknowledge the child internally, even if not claimed in court. This lesson unpacks the condition, its exceptions, and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses whether the master's claim (da'wa) is a condition for establishing the lineage of a child born to his slave woman (umm al-walad). Two realms are considered: judicial (al-qada) and divine (between him and Allah).
The distinction between zahir (externally proven) and batin (inner reality) is key in Hanafi usul.
2
State the Hanafi position on judicial reality
For judicial purposes, the master's claim is absolutely required. Without da'wa, the child's lineage is not established in court. The slave woman does not become a 'bed' (firash) entitling her child to legal paternity.
This is the mufta bihi position as per al-Hidayah and Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Contrast with divine reality
In the divine sphere, if the master had intercourse under conditions that make the child likely his (confinement, no withdrawal), he must acknowledge the child even without judicial claim. If he withdrew or did not confine her, he may deny.
The conditions (tahsine and 'azl) affect the presumption of paternity.
4
Examine the exception for the insane master
An insane master cannot make a claim, yet his istilad (making her umm al-walad) is valid. This suggests an exception, though the text notes it is problematic.
This highlights the principle that intention is not required for the status, but claim is needed for judicial proof.
5
Apply the distinction to a practical scenario
If a master dies without claiming a child, the child's lineage is not judicially recognized unless proven otherwise. However, the slave woman may still be considered umm al-walad for inheritance, as per the inner reality.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the child's inheritance rights depend on judicial lineage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Book of Emancipation, Chapter of Umm al-Walad. The claim: The ruling in the madhhab is based on it because its people explicitly state contrary to it. He said in al-Bada'i: The owned or mudabbara slave woman's child's lineage is not established even if the master confines her and seeks the child from intercourse without the claim (da'wa) according to us because she does not become a bed (firash) without the claim. End. If he intends establishment before the judge externally, they explicitly stated that it is necessary absolutely; but if he intends what is between him and Allah, it is stated in al-Hidayah and others that what we mentioned about the condition of da'wa is only in judicial matters. As for what is between him and Allah, if he had intercourse with her and confined her and did not withdraw, he must acknowledge and claim him because the apparent indicates the child is from him. If he withdrew from her or did not confine her, it is permissible for him to deny him because another apparent counterbalances it. Confinement (tahsine) is preventing her from going out and appearing in places of suspicion; withdrawal ('azl) is having intercourse without ejaculating at the place of copulation. In al-Mujtaba citing Tajrid al-Quduri: The lineage of the slave woman's child from her master is established even if he does not claim him. So this is a text that the master's claim is not a condition for her becoming umm al-walad in reality, but is only required for its manifestation and judicial ruling. End. And in it also: The emancipation of an insane person and his tadbir are not valid, but his istilad is valid. End. Yet claim is not conceivable from him. So if this is correct, it is an exception and it is problematic.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the requirement of the master's claim (da'wa) for establishing the lineage of a child born to a slave woman. The author explains the Hanafi position: For judicial purposes, the claim is absolutely necessary; the slave woman does not become a 'bed' (firash) entitling her child to lineage without an explicit claim from the master. However, in terms of the master's obligation before Allah, if he had intercourse under conditions where the child is likely his (she was confined, no withdrawal), he must acknowledge him. If he withdrew or did not confine her, he may deny. The passage cites al-Bada'i', al-Hidayah, and Tajrid al-Quduri to distinguish between judicial proof and inner reality. The exception for an insane master is noted: his istilad (making her umm al-walad) is valid even without claim, but this is problematic as the rule requires claim. The term 'tahsine' (confinement) means preventing her from going out, and 'azl (withdrawal) means coitus interruptus.

Key Terms
دعوة (da'wa): The master's explicit claim or acknowledgment that the child is his offspring.
أم ولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who gives birth to her master's child, obtaining certain rights including freedom upon his death.
تحصين (tahsine): The master's confinement of the slave woman to prevent her from going out, implying exclusive access.
عزل ('azl): Withdrawal during intercourse to avoid pregnancy (coitus interruptus).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in the passage, what is the difference between judicial and divine reality regarding the master's claim for the lineage of his slave woman's child?
A) The claim is required in both judicial and divine reality.
B) The claim is required only for judicial reality, not for divine reality.
C) The claim is required only for divine reality, not for judicial reality.
D) The claim is not required in either realm.

Key Takeaway
The master's claim is a procedural necessity for judicial lineage, but not for the divine reality where the master must acknowledge his child if conditions indicate paternity.

Master's Denial of Paternity of Umm al-Walad's Child
Explaining the master's ability to deny the child's lineage without li'an, and the effect of prolonged silence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the master can deny paternity of his umm al-walad's child without li'an and identify the condition that prevents denial after prolonged silence.

Core Idea
A weak firash allows denial without li'an; prolonged silence equals acknowledgment and blocks denial.

In Hanafi law, the right to deny paternity depends on the strength of the marital bed (firash). This lesson examines a specific case: the master's denial of a child born to his slave woman (umm al-walad). Because her firash is weak, he can deny without the formal li'an procedure, but only within a certain timeframe. We will unpack the legal reasoning and the effect of prolonged silence as cited from al-Mabsut.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master's denial of paternity of the child of his umm al-walad. The ruling is that the denial is effective without li'an. Understand why: because the firash (bed) of an umm al-walad is weak.
Weak firash: the master can even transfer it by marrying her off.
2
Understand the concept of firash
Firash is the legal relationship that establishes paternity. For a free wife, the firash is strong, so denial requires li'an. For an umm al-walad, it is weak, allowing simple denial.
This is a key distinction in Hanafi law.
3
Apply the rule: explicit and implicit denial
The text says denial can be explicit (sarih) or implicit (dilala). For example, claiming lineage of one child while silent on another implies denial of the latter.
Implicit denial is valid as long as the intention is clear.
4
Analyze the effect of prolonged silence
According to al-Mabsut, if the master remains silent for a prolonged period after the child's birth, he loses the right to deny. Prolonged silence combined with accepting congratulations etc. is deemed acknowledgment.
There is an intra-Hanafi difference on the exact duration (refer to the chapter of li'an).
5
Note the limit: before judicial ruling
The master can only deny before a judge has ruled on the lineage. Once the judge rules, the lineage is binding and cannot be invalidated by the master's denial.
This reinforces the importance of timely action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وانتفى بنفيه) أي انتفى نسب الولد الثاني بنفي المولى من غير توقف على لعان لأن فراشها ضعيف حتى يملك نقله بالتزويج بخلاف المنكوحة حيث لا ينفي نسب ولدها إلا باللعان لتأكد الفراش. أطلق في النفي فشمل الصريح والدلالة كما إذا ولدت ولدين في بطنين فادعى نسب الثاني كان نفياً للأول، وكذا لو كانوا ثلاثة فادعى نسب الثاني كان نفياً للأول، وكذا لو كانوا ثلاثة فادعى نسب الأكبر كان نفياً لما بعده كما قدمناه. وشمل ما إذا تطاول الزمان وهو ساكت بعد ولادته وصرح في المبسوط بأنه إذا تطاول الزمان لا يملك نفيه لأن التطاول دليل إقراره لوجود دليله من قبول التهنئة ونحوه فيكون كالتصريح. واختلافهم في التطاول سبق في اللعان. وصرح في المبسوط أيضاً بأنه إنما يملك نفيه وإذا لم يقض به القاضي، فأما بعد القضاء فقد لزمه بالقضاء فلا يملك إبطاله اهـ.
His statement: (And it is denied by his denial) i.e. the lineage of the second child is negated by the master's denial without waiting for li'an because her bed (firash) is weak to the extent that he can transfer it by marriage, unlike a married woman where the lineage of her child is not denied except by li'an due to the strength of the bed. He made denial absolute, including explicit and implicit, as if she gives birth to two children in two pregnancies and he claims the lineage of the second, it is a denial of the first. Similarly if there are three and he claims the lineage of the second, it is a denial of the first; and if he claims the lineage of the eldest, it is a denial of those after, as we have presented. It also includes if time passes while he remains silent after its birth, and it is stated in al-Mabsut that if time passes, he does not have the right to deny because prolonged silence is evidence of his acknowledgment due to the presence of evidence like accepting congratulations and similar, so it is like explicit statement. Their difference over time lapse preceded in the chapter of li'an. And it is stated in al-Mabsut also that he only has the power to deny if the judge has not ruled; after the ruling, it becomes binding and he cannot invalidate it. End.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the master's right to deny paternity of a child born to his slave woman (umm al-walad). Because her 'bed' (firash) is weak—he can even transfer it by marrying her off—the denial does not require the formal procedure of li'an (mutual imprecation) needed for a free wife whose bed is strong. The denial can be explicit (sarih) or implicit (dilala), e.g., claiming one child while denying another. A key point: if the master remains silent for a prolonged period after the child's birth, he loses the right to deny, because silence with acceptance of congratulations etc. implies acknowledgment. This rule of time lapse (tatawul) is also discussed in the chapter of li'an. Additionally, the master can deny only before a judicial ruling; once a judge has ruled the child's lineage, the denial becomes ineffective.

Key Terms
فراش (firash): The legal 'bed' or marital entitlement that establishes paternity; strong for free wives, weak for slave women.
لعان (li'an): Mutual imprecation between spouses to deny paternity; not required for umm al-walad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What effect does prolonged silence by the master after the birth of his slave woman's child have on his ability to deny paternity?
A) He can still deny because silence is not acknowledgment.
B) He loses the right to deny because prolonged silence is considered acknowledgment.
C) He can only deny if he explicitly states so within a year.
D) He must perform li'an to deny.

Key Takeaway
The master of an umm al-walad can deny paternity without li'an due to weak firash, but prolonged silence or a judicial ruling can bar that right.

Umm al-Walad: Freedom from All Assets upon Master's Death
Stating that the umm al-walad is freed from all assets upon master's death, not from one-third, and creditors have no claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that an umm al-walad is freed from all assets upon her master's death, explain the two proofs (hadith and qiyas on haja asliyya), and differentiate her ruling from that of a mudabbara.

Core Idea
An umm al-walad's right to freedom overrides all debts because the need for offspring is a primary human necessity.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the unique status of the umm al-walad in Hanafi law. Upon her master's death, she is freed from the entire estate—not merely one-third—and creditors have no claim against her. The source text combines a prophetic hadith with a qiyas analogy to shrouding the deceased, establishing that the need for progeny takes precedence over financial obligations. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the evidentiary basis, and the practical scenarios where the ruling applies or is modified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states that an umm al-walad is freed from all wealth upon her master's death, and creditors have no claim. This is the primary hukm being established.
The ruling is absolute: from every part of the estate, not just one-third.
2
Examine the first proof: the hadith
The hadith from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab (mursal) commands the emancipation of ummahat al-awlad and prohibits selling them for debt or limiting them to one-third. This indicates their privileged status.
Although mursal, it is accepted in Hanafi usul as a valid proof.
3
Analyze the second proof: qiyas based on haja asliyya
The need for offspring is analogous to the need for burial (takfin), which takes precedence over debts and inheritance. The 'illa is the fundamental necessity (haja asliyya).
This distinguishes the umm al-walad from a mudabbara, whose tadbir is a bequest for extra needs.
4
Distinguish from tadbir
A mudabbara is freed from one-third because tadbir is a will concerning surplus needs. The umm al-walad's right is based on a primary need, so she is freed from the entire estate.
Also, the umm al-walad is not considered fungible property (mal mutaqawwim) according to Abu Hanifa.
5
Apply the ruling to special cases
The passage covers acknowledgment in health (full freedom) vs. illness (full freedom if child/pregnancy present, otherwise one-third), and legal death (apostasy, joining dar al-harb).
Her possessions upon death belong to the master's estate unless bequeathed, with istihsan for basic clothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وعتقت بموته من كل ماله ولم تسع لغريمه) لحديث سعيد بن المسيب أن النبي عليه السلام أمر بعتق أمهات الأولاد وأن لا يبعن في دين وأن لا يجعلن من الثلث. ولأن الحاجة إلى الولد أصلية فتقدم على حق الورثة والدين كالتكفين بخلاف التدبير فإنه وصية بما هو من زوائد الحوائج، ولأنها ليست بمال متقوم حتى لا تضمن بالغصب عند أبي حنيفة فلا يتعلق بها حق الغرماء كالقصاص بخلاف المدبر لأنه مال متقوم. أطلق في الموت فشمل الحكمي كردته ولحوقه بدار الحرب، وكذا الحربي المستأمن إذا اشترى جارية بدار الإسلام واستولدها ثم رجع إلى دار الحرب فاسترق الحربي عتقت الجارية لما ذكرنا في المدبر؛ كذا في البدائع. وشمل كلامه ما إذا أقر بأنها ولدت منه في الصحة أو في المرض لكن إن كان في الصحة فإنها تعتق من جميع المال، سواء كان معها ولد أو لم يكن وإن كان الإقرار في المرض، فإن كان معها ولد فكذلك الجواب وإلا فهي أم ولده وحكمها كالمدبر تعتق من ثلث المال؛ كذا في شرح الطحاوي. وذكر في المحيط أنه لو قال لأمته في مرضه ولدت مني، فإن كان هناك ولد أو حبل تعتق من جميع المال وإلا فمن الثلث لأنه عند عدم الشاهد إقرار بالعتق وهو وصية. وفي الخانية: وإذا عتقت بموته يكون ما في يدها من المال للمولى إلا إذا أوصى لها به اهـ. وفي المجتبى عن محمد مات مولى أم الولد ولها متاع وعروض ليس لها منها شيء إلا أني استحسن أن أترك لها ملحفة وقميصاً ومقنعة فأما المدبر فلا شيء له من الثياب وغيره اهـ.
His statement: (And she is freed by his death from all his wealth, and she is not subject to his creditors) due to the hadith of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ordered the emancipation of ummahat al-awlad and that they should not be sold for debt and not be made from one-third. And because the need for offspring is fundamental, so it precedes the rights of heirs and debt like shrouding, unlike tadbir which is a will for something from extra needs. And because she is not fungible property such that she is not guaranteed by usurpation according to Abu Hanifa, so the creditors' right does not attach to her like qisas, unlike a mudabbara because she is fungible property. He made death absolute, including legal death like apostasy and joining the dar al-harb, and similarly a musta'min harbi who buys a slave woman in dar al-islam and makes her umm al-walad then returns to dar al-harb and is captured, the slave woman is freed as mentioned for the mudabbara; as in al-Bada'i. His statement includes if he acknowledges that she gave birth from him in health or illness, but if in health she is freed from all wealth whether there is a child with her or not. If the acknowledgment is in illness, then if there is a child with her, the answer is the same; otherwise she is his umm al-walad and her ruling is like a mudabbara, freed from one-third of the wealth; as in Sharh al-Tahawi. And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that if he says during his illness 'She gave birth from me', then if there is a child or pregnancy, she is freed from all wealth; otherwise from one-third because in the absence of evidence it is an acknowledgment of emancipation which is a will. In al-Khaniyya: When she is freed by his death, what is in her hand of wealth belongs to the master unless he bequeaths it to her. End. In al-Mujtaba from Muhammad: The master of an umm al-walad died and she has furniture and goods, she has nothing of them except that I consider it good to leave her a blanket, a shirt, and a head covering. As for a mudabbara, she has nothing of clothes or otherwise. End.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on the emancipation of an umm al-walad upon her master's death. She is freed from the entire estate (not just one-third) because her right is based on the fundamental need for offspring, outweighing heirs and creditors. This is supported by the hadith of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab. Unlike a mudabbara (who is freed upon death but from one-third), the umm al-walad is not considered fungible property (mal mutaqawwim) according to Abu Hanifa, so creditors have no claim on her. The ruling covers various scenarios: if the master acknowledges in health, she is freed from all wealth regardless of a living child; if in illness, and there is a child, same; if no child, she is like a mudabbara (freed from one-third). Also, if the master apostatizes or returns to dar al-harb, she is still freed upon his death. The passage also mentions the ruling on her possessions: upon his death, her possessions belong to the master's estate unless he bequeaths to her, but there is an istihsan (juristic preference) to leave her basic clothing.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who bears her master's child, entitling her to freedom upon his death from all assets.
مدبر (mudabbir/mudabbara): A slave who is promised freedom upon the master's death (tadbir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, from which portion of the estate is an umm al-walad freed upon her master's death?
A) From one-third of the estate like a mudabbara.
B) From the entire estate, regardless of debts.
C) Only if there is a living child.
D) Only if the master was healthy at the time of acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
An umm al-walad's freedom upon master's death takes precedence over all financial claims because it is based on the foundational human need for progeny, supported by a prophetic hadith and analogical reasoning.

The Umm Walad: Freed from All Property and Not Working for Creditors
Core ruling with proof on umm walad's freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two-part Hanafi ruling on the umm walad upon her master's death and explain the primary proof (hadith and qiyas).

Core Idea
The umm walad is freed from all the master's wealth, not from one-third, and is not required to work for creditors.

The status of the umm walad (mother of the master's child) is unique in Hanafi fiqh. Unlike a mudabbar (slave promised freedom after death), she is freed from the entire estate and does not have to serve creditors. This lesson examines the primary evidence and reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the two-part ruling
Identify that the umm walad is (a) freed from all the master's property, and (b) not required to work (sa'y) to pay off the master's debts.
2
Identify the primary proof
Locate the hadith of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab (mursal) that the Prophet commanded freeing ummahat al-awlad and forbade selling them for debt or treating them as from the one-third.
This hadith is the main textual evidence.
3
Understand the wajh al-dalalah
Apply the Hanafi usul principle that the imperative ('amara') indicates obligation (wujub). The command to free and the prohibitions establish the ruling.
Even though the hadith is mursal, it is accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Examine the rational arguments
Note two reasons: (1) the need for the child is fundamental (asliyya), so it takes precedence over debts and heirs; (2) the umm walad is not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) per Abu Hanifa, so creditors have no claim on her, analogous to qisas.
Compare with the mudabbar who is valuable property and subject to the one-third rule.
5
Distinguish from mudabbar
The mudabbar is freed from one-third because tadbir is a will; the umm walad is freed from all because her right is based on the fundamental need of the child.
This distinction is critical for understanding the Hanafi position.

And it is mentioned in al-Muhit that if he said to his slave woman in his illness 'You gave birth from me', then if there is a child or pregnancy, she is freed from all property, otherwise from a third because in the absence of a witness it is a confession of freeing which is a will. And in al-Khaniyya: When she is freed upon his death, whatever wealth is in her hand is for the master unless he bequeathed it to her. And in al-Mujtaba from Muhammad: The master of an umm walad died and she has goods and wares; she has nothing of them except that I think it good to leave her a wrap, a shirt, and a veil. As for the mudabbar, he has nothing of clothes etc. And the author did not mention here the ruling of the child of the umm walad from other than the master because he presented it in the Book of Freeing that the child, i.e., the fetus, follows the mother in istilad. So if the master marries his umm walad to a man and she gives birth, it is in the ruling of the mother because the right to freedom flows to the child like tadbir. Do you not see that the child of a free woman is free and the child of a slave is a slave, and lineage is established from the husband because the bed is his even if the marriage is invalid because the invalid is attached to the valid in rulings. And if the master claims him, his lineage is not established because it is established from another, and the child is freed; as in al-Hidaya. When the master dies, the child of the umm walad is freed like his mother. And in al-Muhit: If one witness testifies that he confessed that she gave birth to this boy from him, and another testifies that she gave birth to this girl from him, the first except by claim. End of the words of al-Shahna. And the apparent meaning of his words, like the author, is that the validity of istilad of the insane and mentally deficient is judicial. And it is possible that the meaning is its validity in religion such that the statement of the trustworthy woman 'and so' etc. is a justification for his statement that she became umm walad in reality, so consider. But it is not hidden that this is a branch of knowledge of intercourse and this is difficult, and is sufficient for that the apparent indications like he prepared her for bedding or not? And this occurs frequently, so his statement (as for) requires scrutiny.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the status of the umm walad (mother of her master's child) upon her master's death. The ruling consists of two parts: (1) she is freed from all of the master's property, not from the one-third share like a mudabbar, and (2) she is not required to work (sa'y) to pay off her master's debts. The proof is the hadith of Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, who reports that the Prophet commanded freeing of ummahat al-awlad and forbade selling them for debt or treating them as from the one-third. The commentator then provides rational arguments: the need for the child is fundamental (asliyya), so it takes precedence over debts and heirs, similar to the shroud (kafan). This differs from tadbir (declaring a slave to be freed after death), which is a will for a superfluous need. Also, the umm walad is not considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) according to Abu Hanifa (she is not liable in tort for usurpation), so creditors have no claim on her, analogous to qisas (retaliation) which cannot be attached by creditors. In contrast, a mudabbar is valuable property. The commentator then discusses sub-rulings: (a) if the master confesses in health that she gave birth from him, she is freed from all property regardless of whether a child is present; (b) if the confession is in illness, and there is a child, same ruling; but if no child, she is like a mudabbar, freed from one-third. (c) Practical details: upon freedom, any property in her hand goes to the master unless bequeathed. (d) The child of an umm walad from another man (e.g., if she was married) follows the mother in freedom and inherits the istilad status; his lineage is from the husband, not the master, and he is freed. (e) The proof of istilad for an insane or mentally deficient master is judicial; it may be established by clear evidence like preparing the woman for intercourse. The passage ends with a note that the commentator will discuss the phrase 'as for the creditor'.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears her master's child; she becomes free upon her master's death from all his property.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave declared to be freed after the master's death; freed from one-third of the property.
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master impregnating his female slave, resulting in her becoming umm walad.
سعى (sa'y): Working to pay off a debt; here, the umm walad is not required to sa'y for the master's debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A verse from the Qur'an
B) A mutawatir hadith
C) A mursal hadith from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab
D) A qiyas from the mudabbar

Key Takeaway
The umm walad is freed from all the master's property and is not required to work for creditors, based on a mursal hadith and rational arguments prioritizing the child's need.

Sub-Rulings of the Umm Walad: Confession and the Status of Her Child
Detailed sub-rulings on umm walad's confession and child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi sub-rulings regarding the umm walad when the master confesses in health vs illness, and determine the status of her child born from another man.

Core Idea
A confession of istilad in health frees the umm walad from all property regardless of a child; in illness, a child must be present for full freedom, otherwise she is like a mudabbar.

The previous lesson covered the core ruling. This lesson delves into important details: what happens when the master confesses during his terminal illness? What if no child is born? And what about the child of the umm walad from another man? These sub-rulings are crucial for a complete understanding.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish confession in health vs illness
If the master confesses in health that she gave birth from him, she becomes umm walad and is freed from all property upon death, even if no child is present. In illness, the confession is treated as a will unless there is a child or pregnancy.
2
Apply the illness confession rule
In illness, if there is a child or pregnancy, she is freed from all property; otherwise, she is treated as a mudabbar and freed from one-third.
Because without a witness, the confession in illness is like a bequest.
3
Identify the child's status
The child of the umm walad from another man (e.g., from a marriage during her slavery) follows the mother in freedom and is also freed upon the master's death. The child's lineage is from the biological father, not the master.
The child is free because the mother is free upon death; the master cannot claim lineage.
4
Note the property in her hands
Upon freedom, any property in her possession belongs to the master unless he bequeathed it to her. She is not entitled to it automatically.
The master may also leave her some items, as in the istihsan of Imam Muhammad.
5
Consider proof of istilad for an insane master
If the master is insane or mentally deficient, istilad can be established by clear evidence, such as preparing her for intercourse. This is a judicial proof.
The text notes this is a frequent issue and requires careful scrutiny.

Commentary

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment; here, the master's acknowledgment that the slave woman bore his child.
وصية (wasiyya): Will or bequest; in Hanafi law, a bequest cannot exceed one-third of the estate unless heirs consent.
لحوق (luhaq): The child's lineage attaches to the biological father.
