Ruling on a Christian's Umm Walad Who Converts to Islam
Explains the sacy obligation for a Christian umm walad converting to Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a Christian's umm walad who converts to Islam and explain the istihsan rationale behind the obligation of sacy.

Core Idea
A Christian's umm walad who converts to Islam must earn her value if her master refuses, balancing benefit to her and harm to him.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific case: the umm walad (slave-mother) of a Christian master converts to Islam. What happens to her status and obligations? The Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to craft a ruling that protects her de facto freedom while safeguarding the master's property rights. We will examine the ruling, its conditions, and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the conversion to Islam of a Christian's umm walad. The ruling is that she must work (sacy) to earn her value (qimah). This is wajib, not fard, because it is based on a judge's ijtihad.
The ruling applies only when the master refuses Islam; if he converts, she remains as she was.
2
Understand the talil (istihsan)
The reasoning balances two considerations: (1) benefit to the slave: she gains de facto freedom ('hurriyatan bi'l-yad') and avoids humiliation; (2) harm to the master: he would lose his property if she were freed while insolvent. The sacy mechanism ensures the master receives compensation.
The author states 'li-anna al-nazara min al-janibayn' (consideration from both sides), a classic istihsan formulation.
3
Recognize the condition for sacy
Sacy is imposed only when Islam is presented to the master and he refuses (ab'a). If he accepts Islam, the umm walad remains as she was, with no obligation to earn. This condition is not explicitly stated in the matn but is added by the commentator.
This condition distinguishes the case from a regular female slave's conversion, where the master is ordered to sell her.
4
Compare with related cases
A regular female slave (qinn) who converts: the master is ordered to sell her, as sale is possible. For an umm walad, sale is prohibited, so sacy substitutes. A mudabbar (slave promised freedom after master's death) who converts is treated like an umm walad: he works for his value.
The analogy shows that when the original property right (sale) is unavailable, a substitute (sacy) is used.
5
Apply the ruling to the child
If the umm walad dies during sacy, her child must continue the payments, just like the child of a mukatabah. The child becomes 'mustas'a' (liable to earn) following the mother.
This extension is based on the principle that the mother is treated as a mukatabah during sacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أسلمت أم ولد النصراني سعت في قيمتها لأن النظر من الجانبين في جعلها مكاتبة لأنه يندفع الذل عنها بصيرورتها حرة يداً والضرر عن الذمي لانبعاثها على الكسب نيلاً لشرف الحرية فيصل الذمي إلى بدل ملكه أما لو أعتقت وهي مفلسة تتوانى في الكسب ومالية أمية الولد يعتقدها الذمي متقومة فيترك وما يعتقده، ولأنها إن لم تكن متقومة فهي محترمة وهذا يكفي لوجوب الضمان كما في القصاص المشترك إذا عفا أحد الأولياء يجب المال للباقين. والمراد بقيمتها هنا ثلث قيمتها لو كانت قنة؛ كذا في غاية البيان. والمزاد بالنصراني الكافر. وترك المصنف قيداً وهو أن محل وجوب السعاية عليها فيما إذا عرض الإسلام عليه فأبى، أما إذا أسلم فهي باقية على حالها. ولم يصرح بأنها في حال السعاية مكاتبة وقد قالوا : إنها مكاتبة لكن إذا عجزت لا ترد في الرق وشرط قاضيخان في الخانية لكونها مكاتبه قضاء القاضي قال : وإذا قضى القاضي عليها بالسعاية كان حالها حال المكاتب ما لم تؤد السعاية. وقال فخر الإسلام ومعنى المسألة أن القاضي يقدر قيمتها فينجمها عليها. وأشار بكونها أم ولده إلى أنه لو مات قبل السعاية عتقت بلا سعاية كما هو حكم الولد، وإلى أن المدبر النصراني إذا أسلم فحكمه حكم أم الولد يسعى في قيمته وهي قيمته لو كان قناً أو الثلثان على ما مر . وقيد بأم الولد لأن القنة للنصراني إذا أسلمت فإن المولى يؤمر بالبيع، وكذا قنه لأن البيع أوجب الحقوق لأن الكاتب ربما يعجز فيحتاج إلى بيعه فصارت الكتابة بمنزلة البدل عن البيع ولا يصار إلى البدل ما دام الأصل مقدوراً عليه ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وقيد مسكين الجبر على البيع بعرض الإسلام عليه فيأبى. وفي المحيط : وإذا قضى القاضي عليها بالقيمة ثم ماتت ولها ولد ولدته في السعاية سعى الولد فيما عليها لأن الولد صار مستسعى . تبعاً لأمه كولد المكاتبة لأنها بمنزلة المكاتبة اهـ.
If the umm walad of a Christian converts to Islam, she works to earn her value, because consideration from both sides makes her like a mukatabah, since humiliation is removed from her by becoming free in hand, and harm from the dhimmi is removed by her striving to earn to attain the honor of freedom, so the dhimmi reaches the replacement of his property. But if she were freed while insolvent, she would be lazy in earning, and the Christian believes the property of umm al-walad to be valuable, so he leaves her and what he believes. Also, if she is not valuable, she is respected, and this suffices for the obligation of compensation, as in joint retaliation when one of the guardians pardons, the money is owed to the others. What is meant by her value here is one-third of her value if she were a slave; thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. By 'Christian' is meant 'unbeliever'. The author omitted a condition, which is that the place of obligation of earning is when Islam is presented to him and he refuses. If he converts to Islam, she remains as she was. He did not state that during the period of earning she is a mukatabah, and they said: She is a mukatabah, but if she fails, she is not returned to slavery. Qadi Khan in Al-Khaniyyah made her being a mukatabah conditional on the judge's ruling. He said: If the judge rules that she must earn, her status is that of a mukatab until she pays the earning. Fakhr al-Islam said: The meaning of the issue is that the judge assesses her value and sets installments upon her. His statement that she is his umm walad indicates that if he dies before the earning, she becomes free without earning, as is the ruling for the child. Also, that if a Christian mudabbar converts to Islam, his ruling is that of the umm walad; he works for his value, which is his value if he were a slave, or two-thirds, as mentioned. He restricted it to umm walad because if a Christian's female slave converts to Islam, the master is ordered to sell her, and similarly his male slave, because selling is the most appropriate of rights, for the mukatab might fail and need to be sold, so kitabah becomes like a substitute for sale, and one does not resort to the substitute as long as the original is available. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. Restriction (miskin) is for the compulsion to sell upon presenting Islam to him and he refuses. In Al-Muhit: If the judge rules the payment of value upon her, then she dies and has a child born during the earning, the child works for what was upon her, because the child became liable to earn following his mother, like the child of a mukatabah, because she is like a mukatabah. End.
Source pages p. 457

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling and rationale (talil) from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning the case of a Christian's umm walad who converts to Islam. The author explains that when the slave girl who has borne a child to her Christian master embraces Islam, she must work to earn her value (sacy). The reasoning is twofold: first, it benefits her by removing humiliation and granting her de facto freedom (hurriyatan bi'l-yad), and second, it protects the dhimmi master's financial interest by allowing her to earn money to replace his lost property. If she were simply freed while insolvent, she would slack off and the master would lose his property. The passage clarifies that the 'value' here is one-third of her market value as a slave, since umm walad cannot be sold and her value is reduced. The commentator notes that the basic text omits a condition: this sacy is only imposed when Islam is presented to the master and he refuses. If the master himself converts, she remains his umm walad without sacy. During the sacy period, she is treated like a mukatabah (slave with contract of emancipation) but with one difference: if she fails to pay, she is not returned to slavery. Qadi Khan requires a judge's ruling to make her a mukatabah. The passage also draws analogies: if the master dies before sacy completion, she becomes free automatically, just as the child does. Similarly, a Christian mudabbar (slave promised freedom after master's death) who converts must work for his value. The text contrasts with a regular female slave who converts: the master is ordered to sell her, because sale is the primary means of recovering value. The passage ends with a case from Al-Muhit: if the umm walad dies during sacy, her child must continue the payments, like the child of a mukatabah, because the child follows the mother. Throughout, the commentary adheres to the Hanafi school's principles, particularly istihsan and consideration of harm (darar).

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, by whom the child's lineage is established, and who becomes free upon her master's death.
سعت (sa'at): She works to earn money, specifically for the purpose of paying her value to the master.
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A slave who has made a contract with her master to earn her freedom by paying installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to justify that compensation is due even if the slave is not considered valuable (muttaqawwamah)?
A) The analogy of sale of a regular slave.
B) The analogy of the child following the mother in sacy.
C) The analogy of joint retaliation (qisas) when one guardian pardons.
D) The analogy of a mudabbar's conversion.

Key Takeaway
A Christian's umm walad who converts to Islam must earn her value if her master refuses Islam, balancing her freedom with his property rights through istihsan.

Claiming Child from Jointly-Owned Slave Woman
Ruling that one who claims a child from a jointly-owned slave woman establishes paternity, making her umm walad and owing half her value and 'aqr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four legal consequences when a co-owner claims a child from a jointly-owned slave woman and explain the Hanafi proof based on the indivisibility of istilad.

Core Idea
Paternity and umm walad status are indivisible; compensation owed to co-owner for his share.

This passage deals with a specific scenario in slave law: when one co-owner of a female slave claims the child she bears. Islamic law had to resolve the tension between ownership rights and the irrevocable status of umm walad. The ruling demonstrates how the Hanafi school applies the principle that paternity (nasab) and the cause of conception (al-‘uluq) cannot be divided, leading to a full transfer of the slave's status and partial financial liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four consequences
The ruling yields four legal results: (a) the child's lineage is established to the claimant, (b) the slave woman becomes his umm walad, (c) he must pay half her value to the co-owner, (d) he must pay half her 'aqr (dower equivalent).
2
Trace the proof for each consequence
For lineage: because conception (al-‘uluq) is indivisible, paternity extends to the whole child. For umm walad: because istilad is indivisible (Abu Hanifa's view). For value: because he acquires the partner's share by completing istilad. For 'aqr: because he had intercourse with a jointly-owned slave.
The proof is based on the nature of the cause (talil).
3
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that she becomes wholly umm walad. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only the claimant's share becomes umm walad, and he must then purchase the partner's share.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the co-owner automatically loses his share and receives compensation. Under the Sahibayn, the co-owner retains his share until the claimant purchases it.
Practical difference in the moment of transfer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولو) ادعى ولد أمة مشتركة ثبت نسبه وهي أم ولده ولزمه نصف قيمتها ونصف عقرها لا قيمته أما ثبوت النسب فلأنه لما ثبت في نصفه لمصادفته ملكه ثبت في الباقي ضرورة أنه لا يتجزأ لما أن سببه لا يتجزأ وهو العلوق إذ الولد الواحد لا يعلق من مائين، وأما صيرورتها أم ولد فلأن الاستيلاد لا يتجزأ عنده وعندهما يصير نصيبه أم ولد له ثم يتملك نصيب صاحبه إذ هو قابل للملك . وأما ضمان نصف القيمة فلأنه تملك نصيب صاحبه لما استكمل الاستيلاد، وأما ضمان نصف العقر فلأنه وطئ جارية مشتركة إذ الملك
His statement: 'And if he claims a child of a jointly-owned slave woman, his lineage is established, and she is his umm walad, and he is liable for half her value and half her 'aqr, not her full value. As for the establishment of lineage, because when it is established in his half, coinciding with his ownership, it is established in the remainder necessarily because it cannot be divided, as its cause (the conception) cannot be divided, for a single child cannot be conceived from two. As for her becoming umm walad, because istilad (having a child by one's slave) cannot be divided in his view (Abu Hanifa), and in the view of his two companions, his share becomes umm walad for him, then he acquires ownership of his partner's share because it is subject to ownership. As for the guarantee of half the value, because he acquired ownership of his partner's share when he completed istilad. As for the guarantee of half the 'aqr, because he had intercourse with a jointly-owned slave girl.'
Source pages p. 458

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's ruling about claiming a child from a jointly-owned slave woman. The scenario: two men co-own a slave girl; one of them claims that a child she bears is his. The ruling has four consequences: (1) The child's lineage is established to the claimant. (2) The slave woman becomes his umm walad. (3) He must pay half her value to the co-owner. (4) He must also pay half her 'aqr (dower equivalent for intercourse). The explanation: Lineage is established even in the co-owner's half because the cause (conception) cannot be partial; a single child cannot be conceived from two men. So paternity necessarily extends to the entire child. For her becoming umm walad, Abu Hanifa holds that istilad (the act of fathering a child on a slave) is indivisible, so she becomes wholly umm walad. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say that only the claimant's share becomes umm walad, but he then acquires the co-owner's share (by paying value) because it is subject to ownership. The guarantee of half the value is because the claimant effectively takes the co-owner's share due to the completion of istilad. The 'aqr (compensation for intercourse with another's property) is because he had intercourse with a jointly-owned slave—note that the 'aqr is half because he only owned half at the time of intercourse. This ruling demonstrates the Hanafi principle that paternity and umm walad status are indivisible, and that financial compensation is owed to the co-owner.

Key Terms
الأمة المشتركة (al-ammat al-mushtarakah): A female slave owned jointly by two or more persons.
العقر (al-'aqr): Compensation paid for having intercourse with a slave woman owned by another; equivalent to the dower for a free woman.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): The act of a master begetting a child from his female slave, which triggers umm walad status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he had intercourse with a slave partially owned by another.
B) Because he must pay for the child's maintenance.
C) Because 'aqr is the full price for the slave.
D) Because he is penalized for lying.

Key Takeaway
When a co-owner claims a child from a jointly-owned slave, the child's lineage and the mother's umm walad status are established wholly, requiring compensation of half value and half 'aqr to the partner.

Dual Paternity for a Claimed Child from a Jointly-Owned Slave-Girl
Commentary on a ruling of dual paternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when both co-owners claim a child from a jointly-owned slave-girl, explain the dalil from Umar and Ali, and distinguish the Hanafi position from opposing schools.

Core Idea
When both co-owners claim the child, lineage is established from both; divisible effects split, indivisible effects are established fully.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a unique Hanafi ruling: when two co-owners of a slave-girl both claim her newborn child, the child becomes the son of both, with full mutual inheritance rights. The proof comes from the practice of the Caliphs Umar and Ali. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its legal reasoning, and the inter-madhhab dispute it generates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the ruling
List all the legal effects mentioned: lineage establishment, umm walad status, 'aqr payment, offsetting, and inheritance. Note that each effect has its own basis.
2
Trace the primary evidence (dalil naqli)
The commentator cites the letter of Umar to Shuraih, with the presence of Companions, and a similar report from Ali. Identify the exact wording and its legal implication.
Also note the use of the passive imperative 'yad'iya' in Umar's statement.
3
Analyse the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The text argues that lineage is indivisible in essence but its legal effects are divisible. Understand the Hanafi principle: what accepts division is split, what does not is established for each fully.
This reasoning responds to the objection that a child cannot have two fathers.
4
Map the inter-madhhab dispute
Other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) hold that lineage cannot be shared, so the child would be assigned to one father. Contrast their proof (indivisibility of lineage) with the Hanafi response (divisible effects).
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: inheritance and mother's status differ.

His saying: (And if) they both claim him together, his lineage is established from them both, and she is the mother of their child, and upon each one is half the 'aqr, and they offset each other, and he inherits from each the inheritance of a son, and they inherit from him the inheritance of a father. As for the establishment of lineage from them both, it is due to the letter of Umar to Shuraih in this incident: 'They were confused, so it was made confusing for them. If they had clarified, it would have been clarified for them. He is their son; he inherits from them and they inherit from him, and he belongs to the one who remains of them.' And that was in the presence of the Companions, and similar is reported from Ali. And because they are equal in entitlement, so they are equal in it. Lineage, although it is indivisible, yet rulings that are divisible are attached to it, so what accepts division is established in their favor in a divided manner, and what does not accept it is established for each one of them in full. His saying: (Then if the mukatib becomes unable, he may sell her. The pronoun in 'he' refers to the partner, because after the mukatib's inability, his disposition becomes ineffective... [page 460] ... [interrupted text] ... completely, without anything else; and the statement of the qa'if is not considered, and the Prophet's pleasure with his statement about Usamah was only because the disbelievers used to cast doubt on Usamah's lineage, so the qa'if's statement cut off their doubt, so he was pleased. As for her being an umm walad for both, it is due to the validity of each one's claim in his share of the child, so his share in her becomes an umm walad for him following his child. As for the obligation of half the 'aqr upon each one, it is due to what we have presented. As for the offsetting, it is because there is no benefit in engaging in collection. The benefit of imposing the 'aqr along with offsetting is that if one of them releases his right, the other's right remains. Also, if the share of one is in dirhams and the other in dinars, he may pay dirhams and take dinars, as in Fath al-Qadir; and if one's share is more than the other's, he takes the excess. As for his inheritance from each of them as a complete son, it is because he acknowledged his entire inheritance to him, and it is evidence in his favor. As for their inheritance from him as a single father, when both are alive, it is due to their equality in lineage, as if they had established evidence.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It explains the ruling when two co-owners of a slave-girl both claim her newborn child as their own. The Hanafi position is that the child becomes the son of both, the slave-girl becomes an umm walad for both, and each must pay half the 'aqr (compensation for loss of value due to childbirth). The two payments offset each other. The child inherits from each co-owner as a full son, and each co-owner inherits from the child as a father. The primary proof is the practice of Caliph Umar and Caliph Ali, documented in a letter to the judge Shuraih. Umar told two men who both claimed a child: 'He is the son of both; he inherits from you and you from him, and he belongs to the survivor.' This occurred in the presence of Companions, indicating consensus. The commentator also explains that although lineage is indivisible in reality, the legal effects can be split: rights that are divisible (like inheritance shares) are split, while indivisible effects (like fatherhood) are established for each fully. Later sections discuss the case of a mukatib (slave with contract of manumission) and the preferences when one co-owner is superior in qualities. The passage also clarifies that the child's inheritance from each is a full son's share, and the co-owners inherit from him as a single father (i.e., each gets half if both are alive).

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master impregnating his own slave-girl, which makes her an umm walad upon childbirth.
عقر ('aqr): Compensation paid to the slave-girl's owner for the loss of her value due to childbirth, typically half her value or a fixed amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Qiyas on the inheritance of siblings
B) The letter of Umar to Shuraih with the presence of the Companions
C) The principle of istihsan
D) The statement of the qa'if

Key Takeaway
When both co-owners claim a child, lineage is established from both, divisible effects split, and indivisible effects are established fully — a Hanafi application of the principle that legal effects can be divided even if the essence is unitary.

Joint Claim of Paternity: Abu Hanifa's Expansive View vs. Sahibayn's Limits
Intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple paternity claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar on multiple paternity claims for a slave, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a scenario involving blood money distribution.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa establishes nasab for all claimants regardless of number; his students impose strict numerical limits.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on a unique case: a slave with unknown lineage is owned jointly, and each owner claims the slave as his son. The ruling affects inheritance, blood money, and family ties. Abu Hanifa's principle that nasab is not divisible leads to an expansive view, while his students, concerned about practical limits, cap the number of recognized fathers. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify the illa behind each ruling and the thamarah al-khilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the baseline case
Identify the scenario: two men own a slave in shares (e.g., 1/10 and 9/10) and both claim paternity. The base ruling: both are recognized as fathers equally, and blood money is divided proportionally among their aqilas.
The slave must have no known lineage; this is a condition for the claim to be valid.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's principle
Abu Hanifa holds that nasab is established from all claimants regardless of number. Understand the underlying rationale: lineage is not a divisible right, so all claims are admitted simultaneously.
This is the default position in the passage: 'يثبت النسب من المدعيين وإن كثروا'.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's objections
Abu Yusuf limits paternity to a maximum of five claimants, Muhammad to three, and Zufar to two. These limits reflect a concern for practical coherence and avoiding absurdity.
Note that Zufar's view is also narrated from Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad, showing internal riwayah differences.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
Work out the practical consequence: if five claimants appear, Abu Hanifa recognizes all five as fathers; Abu Yusuf recognizes up to five but not more; Muhammad recognizes only three; Zufar only two. The blood money burden on aqilas changes accordingly.
The dispute also applies when women claimants are involved, as shown in the extension cases.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Consider which position becomes mufta bihi. Although not stated here, later Hanafi authorities often follow Abu Yusuf's view or Muhammad's. Recognize that the passage records the ikhtilaf without explicit tarjih.
In fatwa practice, the view of Abu Yusuf (up to five) is often relied upon due to its moderation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى صاحب العشر عشر موجب الجناية وعلى الآخر تسعة أعشار موجبها، وكذا أولادها لهما على هذا. ولو أن رجلين اشتريا عبداً ليس له نسب معروف أحدهما عشرة والآخر تسعة أعشاره ثم ادعياه معاً فهو ابنهما لا يفضل أحدهما على صاحبه في النسب، فإن جنى فجنايته على عواقلهما أعشاراً كذا في الظهيرية. وقيد بكونهما اثنين للاختلاف فيما زاد عليهما، فعند أبي حنيفة يثبت النسب من المدعيين وإن كثروا . وقال أبو يوسف : يثبت نسبه من اثنين خمسة ولا يثبت نسبه من الثلاثة . وعند محمد يثبت من الثلاثة لا غير. وقال زفر : يثبت من فقط وهو رواية الحسن بن زياد عن الإمام. وفي غاية البيان: لو تنازع فيه امرأتان قضى به أيضاً بينهما عند أبي حنيفة وعندهما لا يقضي للمرأتين، وكذلك يثبت عند أبي حنيفة للخمس. ولو تنازع فيه رجل وامرأتان يقضي به بينهم عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف ومحمد يقضي للرجل ولا يقضي للمرأتين. وإذا تنازع فيه رجلان وامرأتان كل رجل يدعي هذه المرأة والمرأة لا تصدقه على ذلك فعند أبي حنيفة يقضي بين الرجلين ولا يقضي بين المرأتين .اهـ.
Thus the owner of one-tenth owes one-tenth of the blood money, and the other owes nine-tenths. Similarly, their children are like this. If two men buy a slave with no known lineage, one owning one-tenth and the other nine-tenths, then they both claim the child, it is their son, no one is preferred over the other in lineage. If he commits a crime, the blood money is upon their aqilas in tenths. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah. It is conditioned that they be two, due to disagreement about more than two. According to Abu Hanifa, lineage is established from the claimants even if many. Abu Yusuf said: Lineage is established from two up to five, but not from three. According to Muhammad: it is established from three only. Zufar said: it is established from [two?] only, and this is the narration of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from the Imam. In Ghayat al-Bayan: If two women dispute over him, it is also judged between them according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not judged for the two women. Similarly, according to Abu Hanifa, it is established for five. If a man and two women dispute, it is judged between them according to Abu Hanifa, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is judged for the man and not for the two women. If two men and two women dispute, each man claiming this woman and the woman not confirming him, then according to Abu Hanifa it is judged between the two men and not between the two women. End quote.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the establishment of nasab (lineage) when a slave with unknown lineage is claimed by multiple owners. The author begins with a case: two men own a slave in unequal shares (one-tenth and nine-tenths) and both claim him as their son. The ruling: the child belongs to both equally in lineage, and blood money for his crimes is divided proportionally. The condition that they be two is mentioned because there is disagreement when more than two claim. Abu Hanifa holds that lineage is established from all claimants regardless of number. Abu Yusuf limits it to up to five claimants but not three. Muhammad limits it exactly to three claimants. Zufar restricts it to two claimants only, which is also narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa via Hasan ibn Ziyad. The text then cites Ghayat al-Bayan for cases involving women: if two women claim, Abu Hanifa still establishes lineage for both, while the Sahibayn do not. Similar rulings for a man and two women, and two men and two women, always distinguishing between Abu Hanifa’s expansive view and the restrictions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The key principle: for Abu Hanifa, nasab is not divisible and multiple claims can be sustained simultaneously, while his students require a specific number.

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage or paternity established by acknowledgement or proof.
عاقلة (aqila): The tribal group responsible for paying blood money on behalf of a member.
دعوى (da'wa): Claim or assertion of a right, here specifically claiming paternity.
جناية (jinaya): A crime or offense, here specifically a crime that incurs blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The owners must be two in number.
D) The claim must be made immediately after purchase.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's principle that nasab is established for all claimants regardless of number conflicts with the Sahibayn's numerical limits, illustrating how ijtihad balances certainty and practical coherence.

Rulings on a Co-Owned Umm Walad
Ruling on service, emancipation, and compensation for a co-owned umm walad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings when an umm walad is co-owned, distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions, and explain the indivisibility of her freedom.

Core Idea
An umm walad's freedom is indivisible; co-ownership triggers specific rules of service, emancipation, and compensation.

The umm walad holds a unique status in Hanafi fiqh. When she is co-owned, her rights and obligations become more complex. This lesson dissects the rules for service, emancipation upon death, and voluntary freeing, highlighting the intra-Hanafi difference between Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the woman's status
The woman is an umm walad for both co-owners, so she serves each one day alternately.
Service is divided equally between the co-owners.
2
Analyze death of one co-owner
Upon death of one owner, she becomes free. Abu Hanifa holds no liability or sa'y for the living; Sahibayn require sa'y for half her value if the living owner demands it.
Consent to freedom at death is implied by both.
3
Analyze voluntary freeing by one co-owner
If one owner frees her, she becomes free. Abu Hanifa holds no daman or sa'y; Sahibayn require daman if wealthy, sa'y if poor.
The silent partner's consent is irrelevant per Abu Hanifa.
4
Contrast with a regular slave (qinn)
For a qinn, emancipation is divisible (partial freedom possible). For an umm walad, freedom is indivisible by consensus.
This explains why co-ownership rules differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأفاد بكونها أم ولد لهما أنها تخدم كلاً منهما يوماً وإذا مات أحدهما عتقت ولا ضمان للحي في تركة الميت لرضا كل منهما بعتقها بعد الموت، ولا تسعى للحي أبي حنيفة لعدم تقومها، وعلى قولهما تسعى في نصف قيمتها له. ولو أعتقها أحدهما عتقت ولا ضمان عليه للساكت ولا سعاية في قول أبي حنيفة وعلى قولهما يضمن إن كان موسراً وتسعى إن كان معسراً؛ كذا في فتح القدير. فعلى هذا محل قول الإمام العتق يتجزأ في القنة، أما في أم الولد فعتقها لا يتجزأ اتفاقاً وقد نبه عليه في المجتبى.
And he [the author] indicated that she being an umm walad for both means she serves each one day. When one dies, she becomes free and there is no liability on the living from the deceased's estate because each consented to her freedom after death. She does not have to work to pay for the living according to Abu Hanifa because she has no estimable value. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), she must work to pay half her value to him. If one of them frees her, she becomes free and there is no liability on him to the silent partner and no working to pay according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two, he guarantees if wealthy, and she works to pay if he is insolvent. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. On this basis, the Imam's statement that emancipation is divisible in the case of a qinn (slave), but for an umm walad, her emancipation is not divisible by consensus, and he pointed this out in al-Mujtaba.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage outlines the Hanafi rulings specific to an umm walad (a slave woman who bears her master's child) when she is co-owned by two persons. The author mentions that she serves each owner on alternating days. If one owner dies, she becomes fully free (because the deceased's share is freed upon death) and the surviving owner cannot claim compensation from the deceased's estate because both implicitly consented. According to Abu Hanifa, the freed woman owes nothing to the living owner because an umm walad has no calculable market value. But the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that she must work (sa'y) to pay half her value to the surviving owner. Similarly, if one owner voluntarily frees her, she becomes free. Abu Hanifa says the freeing owner owes nothing to the other and she does not work. The Sahibayn say if the freeer is wealthy, he must pay half the value to the silent partner; if poor, she works to pay. The passage concludes by affirming Imam Abu Hanifa's principle that emancipation (itq) is divisible for a regular slave (qinn) but indivisible for an umm walad by consensus.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thus gaining certain rights and eventual freedom upon master's death.
تجزؤ العتق (tajazzu' al-'itq): The concept that emancipation can be partial or divisible, applied differently to qinn vs. umm walad.
سعاية (sa'y): Working to pay a portion of value when emancipation is incomplete or contested.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if one of two co-owners of an umm walad frees her, what is the result?
A) She is free and the freeer must pay half her value to the other owner
B) She is free and the freeer owes nothing to the silent partner
C) She is not free until both free her
D) She is free but must work to pay half her value

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the umm walad's freedom is indivisible, leading to specific rules when co-owned: she serves each owner, becomes free upon death of one, and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf exists on compensation for voluntary freeing.

Conditions for Umm Walad and Divisibility of Emancipation Among Co‑Owners
Rulings on istilad with differing shares and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a slave woman to become an umm walad when co‑owned, and distinguish the divisibility of emancipation between umm walad, mudabbar, and mukatab.

Core Idea
Umm walad's emancipation is indivisible; mudabbar and mukatab are divisible. Umm walad status requires conception during co‑ownership.

This passage from al‑Bahr al‑Rā’iq clarifies rules when multiple persons own shares of a slave woman. It distinguishes between istilād (making her an umm walad) and mere emancipation through a claim (itq). The key condition for umm walad is that the child must be conceived during the period of joint ownership, not before. The passage also contrasts the indivisible emancipation of an umm walad with the divisible emancipation of a mudabbar (tadbir) and a mukatab (kitāba), with supporting proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for umm walad status
Find the explicit statement that a slave woman becomes umm walad only if she conceived during the co‑owners' joint ownership. This is shown by the requirement that she gives birth at least six months after the purchase.
If purchased pregnant, claim of the child only leads to emancipation (itq), not umm walad.
2
Distinguish istilad from itq
Note the difference: in istilad, the ‘mother’ becomes umm walad and is freed upon the master’s death; in itq, only the child is freed from the time of the claim. The text explicitly calls a claim after pregnancy a ‘claim of emancipation’.
This distinction affects whether the woman remains a slave or becomes an umm walad.
3
Compare divisibility of emancipation
The text states that emancipation of an umm walad is indivisible by consensus, whereas emancipation of a mudabbar and a mukatab is divisible. For mudabbar, if one co‑owner frees him while paying a guarantee, the mudabbar is freed but the walā’ remains shared. For mukatab, the freeing owner’s act is valid and the partner can choose between demanding a guarantee or requiring the mukatab to work (sa‘y) for half the kitāba.
Divisibility means a partial freeing is possible for mudabbar and mukatab, but not for an umm walad.
4
Understand inheritance and guardianship effects
When a child is claimed by all co‑owners, his lineage is established from each, and they inherit from each other as a father would. Guardianship over property is shared (Abū Ḥanīfa and Muḥammad), but each can independently give the child in marriage. This shows that although lineage is indivisible, some of its effects (e.g., property disposal) are divisible.
A practical difference: if one co‑owner dies, the other inherits his entire estate.

Source Text

وفي البدائع : وإن كانت الأنصباء مختلفة بأن كان لأحدهم السدس وللآخر الربع وللآخر الثلث وللآخر ما بقي أنه ابنه من عند فعتقها لا يتجزئ اتفاقاً) لم يتعرض لإعتاق المدبر والمكاتب وتخصيصه بأم الولد يفيد تجزي إعتاق المدبر والمكاتب، أما المدبر فيدل عليه ما قدمه في بابه عند قوله فلا يباع ولا يوهب من أنه لو كان المدبر بين اثنين أعتقه أحدهما وهو موسر وضمن قيمة نصيب شركه عتق المدبر ولم يتغير الولاء لأن العتق ها هنا ثبت من جهة المدبر في الحقيقة لا من جهة المعتق لأن المعتق بأداء الضمان لا يملك نصيب الشريك ها هنا لأن المدبر لا يقبل الانتقال الخ فعدم تغير الولاء أي بقاؤه بين المدبر والمعتق دليل على أنه لم يعتق كله من جهة المعتق وإلا كان الولاء له. أما المكاتب فيدل عليه ما في كافي الحاكم من أنه إذا كاتبا عبدهما ثم أعتقه أحدهما جاز والمكاتب بالخيار إن شاء عجز ويكون الشريك بالخيار بين التضمين وبين السعاية في نصف القيمة والعتق عنده وقال أبو يوسف يضمن نصف قيمته لو موسراً. وقال محمد: يضمن الأقل من نصف القيمة ونصف ما بقي من المكاتبة. وإن لم يعجز حتى عن مال كثير أخذ الذي لم يعتق نصف المكاتبة من ماله والباقي لورثته. فهذا صريح في أن إعتاق المكاتب يتجزى عنده ولذا تخير الشريك بين الاستسعاء والعتق والله أعلم. مات : يثبت نسبه منهم، ويصير نصيب كل واحد من الجارية أم ولد له لا يتعدى إلى نصيب صاحبه حتى تكون الخدمة والكسب والغلة بينهم على قدر أنصبائهم لأن كل واحد يثبت الاستيلاد منه في نصيبه فلا يجوز أن يثبت فيه استيلاد غيره اهـ . اشتراها هو وتستند فالحاصل أن الأنصباء إذا كانت مختلفة فالحكم في حق الولد لا يختلف، فأما الاستيلاد فيثبت لكل واحد منهما بقدر ملكه ؛ كذا في الظهيرية وأطلق المصنف في كونها أم ولد لهما وهو مقيد بما إذا كانت حبلت في ملكهما بأن ولدت لستة أشهر فأكثر من يوم الشراء، أما إذا اشترياها وهي حامل بأن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء فادعياه أو اشترياها بعد الولادة ثم ادعياه فإنها لا تكون أم ولد لهما لأن هذه دعوة عتق لا دعوة استيلاد فيعتق الولد مقتصراً على وقت الدعوة بخلاف الاستيلاد فإن شرطها كون العلوق في الملك الحرية إلى وقت العلوق فيعلق حراً، وكذا لو كان الحمل على ملك أحدهما بالتزوج ثم وآخر فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر من الشراء فادعياه فهي أم ولد الزوج، فإن نصيبه صار أم ولد له والاستيلاد لا يحتمل التجزي عندهما ولا إبقاؤه عنده فيثبت في نصيب شريكه أيضاً، وكذا إذا حملت على ملك أحدهما رقبة فباع نصفها من آخر فولدت يعني لتمام النصف فادعياه يكون الأول أولى لكون العلوق أولى في ملكه؛ كذا ليست كأم ولد لواحد لأنها لو جاءت بعد ذلك بولد؛ لم يثبت نسبه من واحد إلا بالدعوى لأن الوطء حرام فتعتبر الدعوة؛ كذا في المجتبى. وأفاد بقوله «وورثا منه إرث أب» أنه لو مات أحدهما قبل الولد فجميع ميراثه للباقي منهما، وأن الولاية عليه في التصرف مشتركة ولذا قال في الخانية من باب الوصي : رجلان ادعيا صغيراً ادعى كل واحد مشتركة بينهما فإنه يثبت نسبه منهما، فإن كان لهذا الولد مال ورثه من أخ له من أمه أو وهب له أخوه لا ينفرد بالتصرف في ذلك المال أحد الأبوين عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وعند أبي يوسف ينفرد .اهـ. وأما ولاية الإنكاح فلكل واحد منهما الانفراد به، قال في التبيين: النسب وإن كان لا يتجزئ لكن يتعلق به أحكام متجزئه كالميراث والنفقة والحضانة والتصرف في المال، وأحكام غير متجزئة كالنسب وولاية الإنكاح، فما يقبل ستة أشهر من بيع فتح القدير. وهي منهما أنه ابنه من أمة في قوله : (أما إذا اشترياها وهي حامل قال الزيلعي عقب قوله ثبت نسبه منهما : معناه إذا حبلت في ملكهما وكذا إذا اشتريا حبلى لا يختلف في حق ثبوت النسب منهما، وإنما يختلف في حق وجوب العقر والولاء وضمان قيمة الولد حتى لا يجب على كل واحد منهما العقر لصاحبه لعدم الوطء في ملكه ويجب عليه نصف قيمة الولد إن كان المدعي واحداً ويثبت لكل واحد منهما فيه الولاء لأنه تحرير على ما عرف في موضعه ا هـ. وقوله ويجب عليه نصف قيمة الولد» أي وقد اشترياها حبلى بخلاف ما إذا حبلت في ملكهما فادعاه أحدهما فإنه لا يلزمه نصف قيمة الولد. وقوله
And in al-Bada'i: If the shares are different, such that one has a sixth, another a quarter, another a third, and another the remainder — that he is his son from [the time of purchase] — then her emancipation is not divisible by consensus. He did not address the emancipation of the mudabbar and mukatab, and specifying [the ruling] with umm walad indicates the divisibility of emancipation of mudabbar and mukatab. As for mudabbar, what was previously stated in its chapter at his saying 'it cannot be sold nor gifted' indicates: if a mudabbar is between two, and one frees him while wealthy, and guarantees the value of the partner's share, the mudabbar becomes free and the wala does not change because the emancipation here is actually from the mudabbar, not from the emancipator; because the emancipator by paying the guarantee does not own the partner's share here because the mudabbar does not accept transfer. So the wala remaining between the mudabbar and the emancipator proves that he was not entirely freed by the emancipator, otherwise the wala would belong to the emancipator. As for the mukatab, what is in Kafi al-Hakim indicates: if they both make a kitaba with their slave, then one of them frees him, it is permissible, and the mukatab has the option to declare himself incapable, and the partner has the option between demanding guarantee or seeking sa'y in half the value, and the emancipation is valid according to him. Abu Yusuf said: he guarantees half his value if wealthy. Muhammad said: he guarantees the lesser of half the value and half of what remains of the kitaba. If he does not declare incapacity even if he has much wealth, the one who did not free takes half the kitaba from his wealth and the remainder goes to his heirs. This is explicit that the emancipation of the mukatab is divisible according to him, so the partner chooses between istis'a' and emancipation. And Allah knows best. (Page 462) Their lineage is established from them, and the share of each one of the young woman becomes an umm walad for him, not extending to the share of his partner, such that service, earnings, and produce are between them according to their shares, because each one's istilad is established in his share. It is not permitted that the istilad of another be established in it. End quote. ... The summary: when shares differ, the ruling regarding the child does not differ. As for istilad, it is established for each one according to his ownership. Thus in al-Zahiriyyah. The author's statement that she is an umm walad for both is conditional upon her having conceived in their ownership, such that she gives birth six months or more from the day of purchase. If they bought her while pregnant and she gives birth less than six months from the time of purchase, and they claim the child, or they bought her after birth and then claimed him, then she is not an umm walad for them because this is a claim of emancipation, not a claim of istilad. So the child is free restricted to the time of the claim, unlike istilad where the condition is that conception occurred in their ownership; freedom is attached to the time of conception. Similarly, if the pregnancy was in the ownership of one of them through marriage, then another [buys a share?] and she gives birth less than six months from the purchase, and they both claim him, then she is an umm walad of the husband, because his share becomes an umm walad for him, and istilad does not accept division according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) nor its preservation according to him (Abu Hanifa?), so it is established in the partner's share as well. Similarly, if she conceived in the ownership of one of them as a slave, then he sold half of her to another, and she gave birth after completing half [of the sale?], and they both claim him, then the first is more entitled because the conception was in his ownership. ... He [the author] indicated by his statement 'and they inherit from him the inheritance of a father' that if one of them dies before the child, all his inheritance goes to the remaining one, and guardianship over him in transactions is shared. Hence in al-Khaniyya from the chapter of wasiyy: Two men claim a minor, each claiming shared ownership — then his lineage is established from them. If this child has wealth inherited from his maternal brother or given as a gift by his brother, neither of the two fathers can independently dispose of that wealth according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, while according to Abu Yusuf he can act independently. End quote. As for the guardianship of marriage, each of them can act independently. In al-Tabyin: Although lineage is not divisible, it entails divisible rulings like inheritance, maintenance, custody, and disposal of property, and indivisible rulings like lineage and guardianship of marriage. ... His statement: 'If they bought her while pregnant' — al-Zayla'i said after his statement 'his lineage is established from them': meaning if she conceived in their ownership, and similarly if they bought her pregnant, there is no difference regarding the establishment of lineage from them. The difference is regarding the obligation of 'aqr, wala, and guarantee of the child's value. So that each one is not obliged to pay 'aqr to the other because there was no intercourse in his ownership, and he must pay half the value of the child if the claimant is one, and each one has wala over him because it is a liberation as known in its place. End quote. And his statement 'he must pay half the value of the child' means when they bought her pregnant, unlike if she conceived in their ownership and one of them claims him, then he is not obligated half the value.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on various rulings related to istilad (establishing paternity of a child from a slave woman) when slave shares are owned by multiple persons. It begins with a quote from al-Bada'i discussing different share fractions and establishing that emancipation of an umm walad is indivisible by consensus. The author then compares with mudabbar (a slave promised freedom upon master's death) and mukatab (a slave in a contract of kitaba) to show that their emancipation is divisible, using earlier proofs: for mudabbar, if one co-owner frees him while paying guarantee, the mudabbar becomes free but wala remains split; for mukatab, the freeing owner's action is valid and the partner can choose between guarantee or sa'y, with differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Then the text shifts to the condition for a woman to become an umm walad: she must have conceived during the co-owners' joint ownership. If purchased while pregnant or after birth, claiming the child only emancipates him from the time of claim, without establishing istilad. Various scenarios are discussed: pregnancy in one owner's share followed by sale, etc., emphasizing that istilad is proportional to ownership share, but the child's nasab is equal for all co-owners. Later, the text covers inheritance and guardianship for such a child: if one 'father' dies, the other inherits all; guardianship of property is shared (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad) but Abu Yusuf allows independent action; however, each can independently give the child in marriage. The commentary concludes with a clarification from al-Zayla'i on the difference between conception in joint ownership vs. purchase while pregnant regarding 'aqr (dower), wala (patronage), and guarantee of the child's value.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilad): The legal process by which a master claims paternity of a child born to his slave woman, making her an umm walad.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with his master to purchase his freedom.
عقر (aqr): A dower-like payment due to the owner of a slave woman upon intercourse by another.
ولاء (wala): Patronage rights established through emancipation, giving the former master inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two men jointly purchase a slave woman who is already pregnant, and they later claim the child, what is the ruling according to Hanafi law?
A) She becomes an umm walad for both
B) The child is free but the woman does not become umm walad
C) Neither the child nor the woman is affected
D) The child is a slave

Key Takeaway
A slave woman becomes umm walad for co-owners only if conception occurred during their joint ownership; otherwise, claiming the child only emancipates the child without conferring umm walad status.

The Definition and Rulings of Oaths (Yamin)
Defines oath linguistically and technically, lists pillars, conditions, and basic rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of yamin, distinguish constructive vs non-constructive oaths, and identify the conditions, pillars, and basic rulings including the status of suspended oaths and ghamous.

Core Idea
A valid oath is a constructive statement invoking Allah's name; suspended oaths count, but false oaths don't.

This lesson draws from the opening of Kitab al-Ayman in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, where the author defines the oath (yamin) both linguistically and technically. He clarifies what types of statements count as oaths, lists their essential elements, and explains the basic rulings of fulfillment and expiation. Understanding this foundation is crucial before proceeding to detailed cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the linguistic and technical definitions. Note that the linguistic definition includes 'covenant' and 'ila', while the technical definition is a constructive sentence invoking Allah's name or attribute to strengthen a statement or compel action.
The technical definition is the operative one for fiqh rulings.
2
Understand the constructive restriction
The author restricts oaths to 'constructive' statements, excluding suspended divorces and emancipations from being oaths in the real sense because they are not constructive. However, suspended oaths (ta'liq) are included as oaths legally, following the view in al-Bada'i'.
This has practical effect for one who swears not to swear and then uses a suspended divorce.
3
List the rukn and shurut
The pillar (rukn) is the wording (lafz). Conditions include sanity, puberty, Islam, and some add freedom. An additional condition from al-Muhit is that the statement must be capable of truth/falsehood and possibility of birr.
The condition of possibility of birr is disputed: Abu Yusuf disagrees.
4
Distinguish ghamous and ta'liq
The ghamous (false oath about the past) is excluded from the definition because it is a pure sin and not a valid oath. In contrast, suspended oaths are valid because they involve a conditional obligation that can be birr or hanth.
The ghamous requires repentance, not expiation.
5
Recognize the dual rulings
Every valid oath has two rulings: obligation of birr (fulfillment) if possible and not sinful, and obligation of kaffarah (expiation) upon intentional hanth. Both birr and hanth can vary from obligatory to recommended to prohibited depending on the sworn matter.
The ruling of birr/hanth is determined by the nature of the act sworn to or against.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان 270 والميثاق والإيلاء واليمين وخرج بقيد الإنشائية نحو تعليق الطلاق والعتاق فإن الأولى ليست إنشائية فليست التعاليق إيماناً حقيقة . وأما مفهومه الاصطلاحي فجملة أولى إنشائية يقمم فيها باسم الله تعالى أو صفته يؤكد بها مضمون ثانية في نفس السامع ظاهراً، أو يحمل المتكلم على تحقيق معناها فدخلت بقيد الظهور الغموس أو التزام مكروه كفر أو زوال ملك على تقدير ليمنع عنه أو محبوب ليحمل عليه فدخلت التعليقات مثل إن فعل فهو يهودي وإن دخلت فأنت طالق بضم التاء لمنع نفسه وبكسرها لمنعها وإن بشرتني فأنت حرب كذا في فتح القدير . وعرفها في الكافي بأنها عبارة عن تحقيق ما قصده من البر في المستقبل نفياً أو إثباتاً. وعرفها في التبيين بأنها عقد قوي به عزم الحالف على الفعل أو الترك. وفي شرح النقاية بأنها تقوي الخبر بذكر الله تعالى أو بالتعليق وظاهر ما في البدائع أن التعليق يمين في اللغة أيضاً قال : لأن محمداً أطلق عليه يميناً وقوله حجة في اللغة، وذكر أن فائدة الاختلاف تظهر فيمن حلف لا يحلف ثم حلف بالطلاق أو العتاق فعند العامة يحنث، وعند أصحاب الظواهر لا يحنث، وركنها اللفظ المستعمل فيها. وشرطها العقل والبلوغ والإسلام ومن زاد الحرية كالشمني فقدسها لأن العبد ينعقد يمينه ويكفر بالصوم كما صرحوا به وزاد في المحيط ثالثاً وهو كون. الخبر المضاف إليه اليمين محتملاً للصدق والكذب متمثلاً بين البر والهتك فيتحقق حكمه وهو وجوب البر اهـ. وهو صحيح لما سيأتي أن إمكان البر شرط لانعقادها عندهما خلافاً لأبي يوسف كما . في مسألة الكوز. وسببها إلغائي تارة إيقاع صدقه في نفس السامع، وتارة حمل نفسه أو غيره على الفعل أو الترك وحكمها شيئان وجوب البر بتحقق الصدق في نفس اليمين، والثاني وجوب الكفارة بالحنث كذا في المحيط . وهو بيان لبعض أحكامها فإنه سيأتي أن البر يكون واجباً ومندوباً وحراماً، وأن الحنث يكون واجباً ومندوباً. وفي المحيط : والأفضل في اليمين بالله تعالى تقليلها لأن في تكثير اليمين المضافة إلى الماضي نسبة نفسه إلى الكذب، وفي تكثير اليمين المضافة إلى المستقبل تعريض اسم الله تعالى للهتك، واليمين بغيره تعالى إلى مكروه وعند البعض للحديث لا تحلفوا بآبائكم ولا بالطواغيت من كان حالفاً فليحلف بالله أو ليذر ) وقال بعضهم: إذا أضيف إلى الماضي يكره، وإذا أضيف إلى وأقول : فيه بحث أما أولاً فلأن هذا إنما يتم على أن الجملة الثانية المؤكدة إنشائية وهو ممنوع، وأما ثانياً بتقدير التسليم فقد خرج بقوله بعدها فتدبر قوله : (أو التزام مكروه) برفع «التزام» عطفاً على جملة . (۱) (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب التوحيد باب ۱۳ كتاب الأدب باب ٧٤ كتاب الأيمان باب ٤. مسلم في كتاب الأيمان حديث ١ ٣. الترمذي في كتاب النذور باب ،۸ ۹ الموطأ في كتاب النذور حديث ١٤. أحمد في مسنده (۱۱،۸،۷/۲). البحر الرائق / ج /2 ثم المستقبل لا يكره وهو الأحسن لما روي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام لما لاعن بين العجلاني وبين امرأته قال العجلاني إن أمسكتها فهي طالق ثلاثاً ولم ينكر عليه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إلى آخره. وفي التبيين : لا تكره عند العامة وفي الولوالجية من أراد أن يحلف بالله تعالى فقال خصمه لا أريد الحلف بالله تعالى يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ .
The Book of Oaths: 270. Oath (yamin) is defined linguistically as covenant, ila, and yamin, and is restricted by the condition of being constructive, excluding suspended divorces and emancipations as they are not constructive. Its technical definition: a constructive primary sentence in which the name of Allah or His attribute is mentioned to strengthen a secondary content in the listener's mind apparently, or to compel the speaker to fulfill its meaning. Included by the restriction 'apparently' is the false oath (ghamus) or undertaking something disliked, disbelief, or loss of ownership on a condition to prevent or compel, thus including suspended oaths like 'If you do X, you are a Jew' etc. In al-Kafi: it is realization of what one intends of righteousness in the future, negatively or affirmatively. In al-Tabyin: it is a strong contract by which the swearer resolves to do or not do something. In Sharh al-Nuqayah: it strengthens the statement by mentioning Allah or by suspension. The apparent view in al-Bada'i' is that suspension is also an oath linguistically. Its pillar is the wording used. Its conditions: sanity, puberty, Islam; some add freedom. An additional condition in al-Muhit is that the news to which the oath is attached must be capable of truth or falsehood, between righteousness and breaking, so its ruling is the obligation of righteousness. This is correct because possibility of righteousness is a condition for its validity according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. Its cause is sometimes to confirm truth in the listener's mind, sometimes to compel oneself or others to act or refrain. Its ruling is twofold: obligation of righteousness upon confirming truth in the oath, and obligation of expiation upon breaking it. This explains some of its rulings, as righteousness can be obligatory, recommended, or prohibited, and breaking can be obligatory or recommended. In al-Muhit: The best regarding an oath by Allah is to minimize it because excessive oaths about the past involve accusation of lying, and about the future involve exposing Allah's name to disrespect. Oaths by other than Allah are disliked according to some due to the hadith: 'Do not swear by your fathers nor by idols; whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent.' Some say: if it refers to the past it is disliked; if to the future it is not disliked, and this is better based on the story of the curse between al-‘Ajlani and his wife. In al-Tabyin: it is not disliked according to the majority. In al-Walwaljiyya: if someone wants to swear by Allah and the opponent says 'I do not want an oath by Allah', he fears disbelief.
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage from the opening of Kitab al-Ayman in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a comprehensive definition of the oath (yamin). The author first distinguishes between constructive and non-constructive statements, clarifying that suspended divorces and emancipations are not oaths in the real sense. He then gives the technical definition: a constructive statement invoking Allah's name or attribute to strengthen a claim or compel action. The passage explains that the oath must be 'apparent' (zahir) to exclude the false oath (ghamus) which is a deliberate lie about the past. It also includes obligations (iltizam) of disliked acts, disbelief, or loss of ownership to prevent or compel. The author cites multiple sources: al-Kafi defines it as realization of righteousness in the future; al-Tabyin as a strong contract of resolve; Sharh al-Nuqayah as strengthening a statement. Importantly, the view from al-Bada'i' is that suspension (ta'liq) is also an oath linguistically, and this has practical implications for one who swears not to swear and then swears by divorce or emancipation. The pillar (rukn) is the wording. Conditions include sanity, puberty, Islam, and some add freedom. An additional condition from al-Muhit is that the attached statement must be capable of truth or falsehood. The rulings are: obligation to fulfill the oath (birr) and expiation (kaffarah) upon breaking. The author notes that birr and hanth can vary in obligation. Finally, the passage discusses the virtue of minimizing oaths by Allah, and the ruling on oaths by other than Allah: disliked by some, but not disliked by the majority of Hanafis, especially when used by way of suspension for deterrence.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): A constructive statement invoking Allah's name or attribute to strengthen a claim or compel action, including suspended oaths.
غموس (ghamus): An oath about the past with deliberate falsehood; it is a major sin and not a valid oath.
بر (birr): Fulfilling the oath by doing what was sworn to do or refraining from what was sworn not to do.
حنث (hanth): Breaking the oath by acting contrary to the sworn statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is included in the Hanafi definition of a valid oath (yamin)?
A) A false statement about a past event (ghamus)
B) A suspended (conditional) statement like 'If I enter, you are divorced'
C) A statement of intention without invoking Allah's name
D) A promise made without any mention of Allah or conditions

Key Takeaway
A valid oath is a constructive statement invoking Allah's name, includes suspended oaths, and its rulings depend on possibility and sinfulness of the sworn matter.

Ruling on Oaths by Other Than Allah: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of oaths by other than Allah, with focus on ta'liq al-talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling of oaths by other than Allah, particularly the use of ta'liq al-talaq wal-itaq, and state the mufta bihi position with its evidence.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths by suspending divorce are not disliked because they provide a deterrent, even though the literal hadith warns against swearing by other than Allah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a nuanced disagreement within the Hanafi school. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that an oath by other than Allah is disliked, but the commentator clarifies that the majority of Hanafis do not consider it disliked, especially when it serves as a security (wathiqah) like suspending divorce or emancipation. Understanding this dispute helps in applying the correct ruling in contemporary contexts where such oaths are common.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the majority (al-'ammah) ruling: oaths by other than Allah, when they serve as a deterrent (e.g., ta'liq al-talaq), are not disliked and are permissible.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
A minority of Hanafis (al-ba'd) consider such oaths disliked (makruh), based on the general prohibition in hadith.
The hadith: 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opponent takes the hadith literally, applying it to all forms of swearing by other than Allah, including suspensions.
They do not distinguish between different types of oaths.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The majority restrict the hadith to oaths without deterrent effect (e.g., 'by your father') and argue that ta'liq is not an oath etymologically but achieves the same purpose, hence not covered by the hadith.
They also cite the Prophet's tacit approval of similar suspensions in the case of al-'Ajlani.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the majority view, such suspensions are valid oaths that take effect upon violation; under the minority view, they are disliked but still valid?
The debate affects whether one should avoid such expressions; the majority sees no issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (واليمين بغير الله تعالى مكروهة) هذا شامل لما فيه حرف القسم وما ليس فيه كالتعليق بالطلاق والعتاق وظاهر ما سيأتي قريباً من قوله . وفي التبيين : لا تكره عند العامة شامل للنوعين لكن في الفتح ما يفيد تخصيصه بالتعليق حيث قال : ثم قيل يكره الحلف بالطلاق والعتاق لقوله صلى الله تعالى عليه وسلم من كان حالفاً فليحلف بالله» الحديث والأكثر على أنه لا يكره لأنه لمنع نفسه أو غيره، ومحمل الحديث غير التعليق مما هو بحرف القسم اهـ. وهو موافق لما سيأتي عن تتمة .الفتاوى قال علي الرازي: أخاف على من قال بحياتي يكفر . ثم راجعت عبارة التبيين فوجدتها تفيد ما قلنا ونصها واليمين بغير الله تعالى أيضاً مشروع وهو تعليق الجزاء بالشرط وهو ليس بيمين وضعاً وإنما يميناً عند الفقهاء لحصول سمي معنى اليمين بالله تعالى وهو الحمل أو المنع واليمين بالله تعالى لا تكره وتقليله أولى من تكثيره . واليمين بغيره مكروهة عند البعض للنهي الوارد فيه، وعند عامتهم لا تكره لأنه يحصل بها الوثيقة لا سيما في زماننا، وما روي من النهي محمول على الحلف بغير الله تعالى لا على وجه الوثيقة كقولهم وأبيك ولعمري ونحوه انتهت أي فإن قوله وأبيك ولعمري لا يفيد الوثيقة فإنه لا يلزم الحالف به بشيء بخلاف التعليق بالطلاق ونحوه فإنه يفيد الوثيقة فإن الحالف إذا حنث يلزمه الطلاق ونحوه فتثق بمن حلف لك به تأمل. لكن سيذكر المصنف من جملة ألفاظ اليمين المنعقدة قوله «العمر الله» وحينئذ فيلزمه بالحنث الكفارة مثل قوله «والله فيفيد الوثيقة إلا أن يفرق بين «لعمري» و«العمر الله» فليتأمل. وذكر القهستاني أن قول المصنف لعمر الله للاحتراز عن قولنا لعمر فلان» لأنه لا يجوز أن يخلف بغيره تعالى، وإذا حلف ليس له أن يبر بل يجب أن يحنث فإن البر فيه كفر عند بعضهم كما في
His statement: 'And the oath by other than Allah is disliked' includes what contains the particle of oath and what does not, like suspension (ta'liq) by divorce and emancipation. In al-Tabyin: it is not disliked according to the majority, including both types. But in al-Fath, there is an indication of its specification to suspension, where it is said: It is disliked to swear by divorce and emancipation due to the hadith 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent,' but the majority hold it is not disliked because it is to prevent oneself or others. The hadith is interpreted as applying to oaths with the particle of oath, not to suspension. This is consistent with what will come from Tatimmat al-Fatawa. 'Ali al-Razi said: I fear for one who says 'By my life' that he disbelieves. Then I reviewed the text of al-Tabyin and found it supports our statement: 'And the oath by other than Allah is also legislated; it is a suspension of a consequence upon a condition, and it is not an oath etymologically, but it is called an oath by the jurists because it achieves the meaning of an oath by Allah, which is compulsion or prevention. The oath by Allah is not disliked, and minimizing it is better. The oath by other than Him is disliked according to some due to the prohibition, but according to the majority it is not disliked because it provides security (wathiqah), especially in our time. What is reported of prohibition is interpreted as swearing by other than Allah not for the purpose of security, like saying 'By your father' or 'By my life' etc.' This means that 'By your father' and 'By my life' do not provide security because the swearer is not bound by anything, unlike suspension by divorce etc., which provides security because if the swearer breaks the oath, divorce occurs. So you trust the one who swears to you by that. But the author will mention among the valid words of oath the phrase 'By the life of Allah' (la-‘amr Allah), and then upon breaking, expiation is due, like 'Wa-Allah'. This provides security, but a distinction must be made between 'la-‘amri' and 'al-‘amr Allah'. Al-Qahastani mentioned that the author's phrase 'by the life of Allah' is to avoid saying 'by the life of so-and-so' because it is not permissible to swear by other than Him. If one swears by other than Him, he should not fulfill the oath but must break it, because fulfilling it would be disbelief according to some.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi discussion on the ruling of oaths by other than Allah, focusing particularly on the suspension (ta'liq) of divorce and emancipation. The author begins by explaining that the matn phrase 'And the oath by other than Allah is disliked' (makraha) is general, covering both oaths with the particle of oath (like 'bi-llahi') and those without, such as suspending divorce or emancipation on a condition. He then cites al-Tabyin, which states that the majority (al-‘ammah) do not consider it disliked. He notes that in al-Fath, there is an indication that the dislike is specific to suspension, but the majority reject this and hold it not disliked because it serves as a deterrent (wathiqah). The hadith 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent' is interpreted as applying to oaths with the particle of oath, not to suspension. The author then quotes ‘Ali al-Razi who feared disbelief for one who says 'By my life', but al-Tabyin clarifies that oaths by other than Allah are still legislated (mashru‘) when they serve as a suspension for deterrence; they are not oaths etymologically but are called oaths due to achieving the same purpose (haml or man‘). The majority (al-‘ammah) do not consider them disliked because the prohibition is understood to apply only to oaths that do not provide security, like 'by your father' or 'by my life', which do not bind the swearer. In contrast, suspending divorce or emancipation provides real security because the swearer faces a consequence. The passage ends by noting that the author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) includes 'bi-‘amr Allah' (by the life of Allah) among valid oath phrases, and that breaking it requires expiation like swearing by Allah. But a distinction is necessary between 'la-‘amri' (by my life) and 'al-‘amr Allah' (by the life of Allah). Al-Qahastani explains that the author's wording 'la-‘amr Allah' is to avoid swearing by other than Allah. If one swears by other than Allah, he must not fulfill the oath but must break it, as fulfilling it could be disbelief according to some.

Key Terms
يمين بغير الله (yamin bi-ghayr Allah): An oath that does not invoke Allah's name, especially suspension of divorce or emancipation to compel or prevent an action.
وثيقة (wathiqah): Security or reliability; the binding effect of an oath that compels the swearer to fulfill or deters them.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending a legal effect (like divorce) on a condition; used as a form of oath to deter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the ruling of swearing by suspending divorce on a condition?
A) It is haram and invalid.
B) It is makruh tahrimi and requires repentance.
C) It is not disliked and serves as a valid deterrent oath.
D) It is only permissible if the condition is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that oaths by other than Allah in the form of suspending divorce or emancipation are not disliked because they serve as a valid deterrent, and this is the mufta bihi position.

Three Types of Oaths in Hanafi Fiqh: Ghamoos, Laghw, Mun'aqidah
Ruling and classification of three oath types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the three types of oaths (ghamoos, laghw, mun'aqidah), state their rulings, and distinguish between them based on time (past/present/future) and intention.

Core Idea
An oath's validity hinges on its object (past/present/future) and the speaker's knowledge and intent.

The Hanafi school classifies oaths into three types, each with distinct rulings: deliberate false oaths about the past plunge the swearer into sin; mistaken oaths about the past carry no penalty; and binding oaths about the future require expiation if broken. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for applying the correct legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal scope and knowledge
Determine whether the oath refers to a past, present, or future event and whether the speaker knows the truth or is mistaken.
Ghamoos and laghw are only about the past; mun'aqidah is about the future.
2
Classify according to the three categories
Use the criteria: ghamoos = deliberate falsehood about the past; laghw = mistaken belief about the past; mun'aqidah = binding promise about the future.
3
Apply the ruling for each type
Ghamoos is a major sin but not a valid oath; laghw has no sin or expiation; mun'aqidah requires expiation if broken.
The Hanafi position: ghamoos is metaphorically called an oath but is not legally binding.
4
Handle edge cases: present time oaths
An oath about the present is effectively about the past by the time the utterance is complete; thus it falls under ghamoos or laghw depending on intent.
Derived from the technical explanation in Sharh al-Wiqayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وفي التبيين : لا تكره عند العامة وفي الولوالجية من أراد أن يحلف بالله تعالى فقال خصمه لا أريد الحلف بالله تعالى يخشى عليه الكفر اهـ . قوله : (فحلفه على ماض كذبا عمدا غموس بيان لأنواعها وهي ثلاثة كما
